Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Introduction to Fasting: Definitions, Rukn, and Causes of Obligation
Opening of Kitab al-Sawm with definitions and sabab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between sawm and siyam, state the rukn of fasting, and identify the different causes (asbab) for various types of fasting, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on Ramadan's sabab.

Core Idea
Fasting's cause varies by type: vow triggers a one-day fast, siyam triggers three; Ramadan's cause is witnessing a part of the month, but scholars differ on whether it includes nights.

This lesson opens the Book of Fasting in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It covers three essential foundations: the precise definitions of sawm and siyam, the rukn (pillar) of fasting, and the causes (asbab) that make different types of fasting obligatory. We also encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute on the exact cause of Ramadan fasting, demonstrating how even within the school, nuanced ikhtilaf exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions of sawm
Begin by understanding that sawm linguistically means refraining from eating; legally it is the specific abstention from dawn to sunset with intention. Note the metaphorical usage for a horse that refrains from grazing.
The author also contrasts 'sawm' with 'siyam' based on a citation from Fatawa al-Zuhayriyya.
2
Distinguish sawm from siyam in vows
According to the text, if someone vows 'alayya sawm', he must fast one day; if he vows 'alayya siyam', he must fast three days. This distinction is supported by Quran 2:196, where 'siyam' is used for a three-day ransom.
This is a Hanafi-specific ruling; other schools may treat them as synonyms.
3
State the rukn of fasting
The rukn (pillar) is the specific abstention (imsak makhsus), which is the essential reality of the act. Without it, the fast is invalid.
In Hanafi usul, the rukn is what constitutes the act; conditions and wajibat are separate.
4
Classify the causes (asbab) for different fasts
The sabab for a vowed fast is the vow itself; for expiatory fasts (kaffarat), the cause is the triggering act (e.g., breaking an oath, killing, zihar, breaking fast); for Ramadan, the cause is witnessing a part of the month.
For Ramadan, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the exact nature of the sabab.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on Ramadan's sabab
Al-Sarakhsi holds that the cause is absolute witnessing of the month (including days and nights). Al-Dabusi, Fakhr al-Islam, and Abu al-Yusr hold that the cause is only the days (the indivisible part of each day). The practical difference lies in whether nights can be considered as part of the cause, affecting intention or qada.
No explicit tarjih is given in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم أخر عن الزكاة وإن كان عبادة بدنية مقدمة على المالية لقرانها بالصلاة في آيات كثيرة، وذكر محمد رحمه الله الصوم عقب الصلاة في الجامع الكبير والصغير نظراً لما قلنا. وهو في اللغة ترك الإنسان الأكل وإمساكه عنه ثم جعل عبارة عن هذه العبادة المخصوصة. ومن مجازه صام الفرس على آريه إذا لم يعتلف ومنه قول النابغة خيل صيام» كذا في المغرب. وفي الشرع ما سيذكره المصنف ولو قال كتاب الصيام لكان أولى لما في الفتاوي الظهيرية : ولو قال الله علي صوم فعليه صوم يوم واحد ولو قال فعلي صيام عليه صيام ثلاثة أيام كما في قوله تعالى ففدية من صيام) [البقرة : ١٩٦] اهـ. وركنه حقيقته الشرعية التي هي الإمساك المخصوص، وسببه مختلف ففي المنذور النذر ولذا قلنا : لو نذر صوم شهر بعينه كرجب أو يوماً بعينه فصام غيره أجزأ عن المنذور لأنه تعجيل بعد وجود السبب وفيه خلاف محمد كما في المجمع وصوم الكفارات سببه ما يضاف إليه من الحنث والقتل والظهار والفطر، وسبب رمضان شهود جزء من الشهر اتفاقاً لكن اختلفوا فذهب السرخسي إلى أن السبب مطلق شهود الشهر حتى استوى في السببية الأيام والليالي وذهب الدبوسي وفخر الإسلام وأبو اليسر إلى أن السبب الأيام دون الليالي أي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ من كل يوم سبب لصوم ذلك اليوم كتاب الصوم قوله : ( على آريه قال الرملي الآري المعلف قال في مختار الصحاح : ومما يضعه الناس في غير موضعه قولهم للمعلف آري وإنما الآري محبس الدابة وفي الصحاح : وهو في التقدير فاعول والجمع أواري قوله : (لما في الفتاوى الظهيرية الخ) قال في النهر : لعل وجهه أنه أريد بلفظ صيام في لسان الشارع ثلاثة أيام فكذا في النذر خروجاً عن العهدة بخلاف صوم، وتوهم في البحر أن الصيغة لها دلالة على التعدد ولا شك أن الصوم له أنواع ثلاثة فادعى أن الأولى صيام وهو ممنوع فقد قال القاضي في تفسيره الآية بيان الجنس الفدية، وأما قدرها فبينه عليه الصلاة والسلام في حديث كعب. فإن قلت: صرحوا بأن صياماً جاء جميعاً لصائم قلت : هذا لا يصح مراداً في الآية ولا في الترجمة كما يدركه الذوق السليم والطبع المستقيم على أن «أل الداخلة على الجمع تبطل معنى الجمعية
The Book of Fasting. It is placed after zakat even though it is a bodily act of worship, which is given priority over financial acts, because of its pairing with prayer in many verses. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned fasting after prayer in al-Jami' al-Kabir and al-Jami' al-Saghir, considering what we said. In language, it is a person's refraining from eating and withholding from it; then it was made an expression for this specific act of worship. Among its metaphorical uses is 'the horse fasted at its tether' when it does not graze, and from it is the saying of al-Nabigha 'fasting horses' as in al-Mughrib. In sacred law, it is what the author will mention. If he had said 'Kitab al-Siyam' it would have been better, because in al-Fatawa al-Zuhayriyya: if someone says 'upon me is sawm', he must fast one day, and if he says 'upon me is siyam', he must fast three days, as in His statement 'a ransom from siyam' [2:196]. End. Its rukn (pillar) is its sacred law reality, which is the specific abstention. Its cause differs: in the vowed fast, it is the vow; hence we said: if he vows to fast a specific month like Rajab or a specific day, and fasts another, it suffices for the vowed one because it is acceleration after the existence of the cause, and there is disagreement from Muhammad as in al-Majma'. The fasting of expiations (kaffarat) has a cause related to it: breaking an oath, killing, zihar, and breaking the fast. The cause of Ramadan, by agreement, is witnessing a part of the month, but they differed: al-Sarakhsi went that the cause is the absolute witnessing of the month, so that days and nights are equal in causation; al-Dabusi, Fakhr al-Islam, and Abu al-Yusr went that the cause is the days without the nights, i.e., the indivisible part of each day is the cause for fasting that day. The Book of Fasting. His statement: 'on its tether' – al-Ramli said: al-Ari is the manger. In Mukhtar al-Sihah: Among things that people place incorrectly is their saying for the manger 'ari', but 'ari' is only the horse's tether, and in al-Sihah: it is in the measure of fa'ul, plural awari. His statement: 'because in al-Fatawa al-Zuhayriyya etc.' – al-Nahr said: perhaps its reason is that the word siyam in the language of the Lawgiver intends three days, so similarly in vows, to exit responsibility, unlike sawm. And in al-Bahr, he imagined that the form indicates multiplicity, and there is no doubt that sawm has three types, so he claimed that siyam is more appropriate, but this is prevented, for the Qadi said in his interpretation that the verse explains the genus of ransom, and as for its amount, the Prophet (peace be upon him) explained it in the hadith of Ka'b. If you say: they explicitly stated that siyam came collectively for a faster, I say: this is not correct intended in the verse nor in the translation, as sound taste and upright nature perceive, since the definite article 'al' entering upon the plural negates the meaning of plurality.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage begins the Book of Fasting (Kitab al-Sawm) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The author first explains why the book is placed after Zakat: even though fasting is a bodily act of worship (normally prioritized over financial acts), it is placed after because of its frequent pairing with prayer in the Quran, and Imam Muhammad placed it after prayer in his compilations. Then the linguistic definition: 'sawm' means refraining from eating, and metaphorically used for a horse that stops grazing. The legal definition (what the author will give) is the specific abstention. The author notes it would have been better to title the book 'Kitab al-Siyam' because of a practical difference: according to Fatawa al-Zuhayriyya, if someone vows 'alayya sawm', he must fast one day; if 'alayya siyam', he must fast three days, as in Qur'an 2:196 (fidyatun min siyam). The rukn (pillar) is the legal reality of specific abstention. The sabab (cause) differs: for a vowed fast, it is the vow itself; for expiatory fasts (kaffarat), the cause is the act triggering expiation (breaking an oath, killing, zihar, breaking fast); for Ramadan, by consensus the cause is witnessing a part of the month, but there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Sarakhsi holds that the cause is the mere sighting of the month (including days and nights), so the obligation is uniform; al-Dabusi, Fakhr al-Islam, and Abu al-Yusr hold that the cause is only the days (the indivisible part of each day), so each day has its own cause. The passage then includes commentary on linguistic points: the word 'ari' (tether/manger) and the distinction between sawm and siyam. The later commentary refutes the claim that 'siyam' always indicates multiplicity, using Quranic analysis and hadith.

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): Linguistically: refraining from eating. Legally: the specific abstention from dawn to sunset with intention.
صيام (siyam): Linguistically similar to sawm; in legal usage, often indicates three days of fasting, as in Qur'an 2:196.
ركن (rukn): The integral reality of fasting: the specific abstention (imsak) from dawn to sunset.
سبب (sabab): The cause that triggers the obligation of fasting: for Ramadan, witnessing a part of the month; for vows, the vow; for expiation, the triggering act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rukn of fasting?
A) The intention before dawn.
B) The specific abstention from dawn to sunset.
C) The refrain from food, drink, and marital relations.
D) The sighting of the moon.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes sawm (one day) from siyam (three days) in vows, and the rukn of fasting is the specific abstention; the sabab of Ramadan is disputed within the school.

Sawm — Fasting

Dispute on the Cause of Fasting Obligation for the Insane
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the sabab of fasting for the insane

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the cause (sabab) of fasting obligation for an insane person who recovers briefly, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to determine whether qada is required.

Core Idea
Witnessing any part of Ramadan triggers obligation for the whole month—but al-Sarakhsi insists each day needs its own cause.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a subtle yet consequential intra-Hanafi disagreement about the sabab (cause) of fasting obligation. The dispute arises when an insane person regains consciousness only briefly during Ramadan. The outcome determines whether he must make up all missed fasts (qada). By understanding both positions and the reconciliation attempted by al-Hidayah, you will grasp how the Hanafi school manages internal divergence.

Guided Path
1
State the two Hanafi positions
Identify the two views: (1) Al-Sarakhsi holds that each day with its preceding night constitutes the cause of obligation; (2) The majority (including the author of al-Hidayah) hold that witnessing any part of the month is sufficient to establish the cause for the entire month.
The dispute is not about the obligation of fasting per se, but about what triggers that obligation for an insane person.
2
Identify the opposing scholar and his proof
Al-Sarakhsi argues that the night is not a valid time for fasting, so consciousness during a night alone cannot establish the cause. The cause must be a full day with its night.
This reasoning is talil (teleological) about the nature of the cause.
3
Identify the majority proof
The majority (including al-Siraj al-Hindi) argue that the month is a single entity; witnessing any part of it establishes the cause for the whole month. This is supported by the principle that the obligation of Ramadan is based on the month itself.
The majority rely on the holistic nature of the month.
4
Analyze the reconciliation by al-Hidayah
Al-Hidayah attempts to reconcile the two views by stating that witnessing part of the month is the cause for the month's obligation, while each day is a separate cause for its performance. This means there is no contradiction but a difference in focus.
The author of al-Hidayah ultimately chooses the majority view.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a person recovers on the first night and then remains insane for the rest of the month, according to al-Sarakhsi no qada is required (because no full day with its night was witnessed), while according to the majority qada is required (because witnessing part of the month suffices for the whole).
This directly affects the mufti's ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اليوم فيجب صوم جميع الأيام مقارناً إياه، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر فيمن أفاق في أول ليلة من الشهر ثم جن قبل أن يصبح ومضى الشهر وهو مجنون ثم أفاق فعلى قول السرخسي يلزمه القضاء ولو لم يتقرر السبب في حقه بما شهد من الشهر حال إفاقته لم يلزمه، وعلى قول غيره لا يلزمه القضاء، وصححه السراج الهندي في شرح المغني لأن الليل ليس بمحل للصوم فكان الجنون والإفاقة فيه سواء، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو أفاق ليلة في وسط الشهر ثم أصبح مجنوناً، وكذا لو أفاق في آخر يوم من رمضان بعد الزوال، وجمع في الهداية بين القولين بأنه لا منافاة فشهود جزء منه سبب لكله ثم كل يوم سبب وجوب أدائه . غاية الأمر أنه تكرر أن صاحب الهداية يختار غير قول السرخسي لأن السرخسي يقول كل يوم مع ليلته سبب للوجوب لا اليوم وحده وتمام تقريره في الأصول.
So fasting all days is obligatory concurrent with it, and the fruit of the dispute appears in one who regains consciousness on the first night of the month, then becomes insane before dawn, and the month passes while he is insane, then he recovers: according to al-Sarakhsi's view, qada is obligatory for him, but if the cause [of obligation] is not established with respect to him by what he witnessed of the month during his consciousness, it is not obligatory. According to another's view, qada is not obligatory for him, and al-Siraj al-Hindi in Sharh al-Mughni authenticated this because the night is not a place for fasting, so insanity and consciousness are equal in it. Based on this dispute, if he regained consciousness one night in the middle of the month then became insane at dawn, and likewise if he regained consciousness on the last day of Ramadan after noon. And al-Hidayah reconciled the two statements: there is no contradiction—witnessing part of it is a cause for the whole, then each day is a cause for the obligation of its performance. The point is that the author of al-Hidayah prefers a view other than al-Sarakhsi's, because al-Sarakhsi says each day with its night is the cause of obligation, not the day alone. The full explanation is in Usul.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the cause of obligation (sabab al-wujub) for fasting in Ramadan. The discussion centers on a person who was insane for most of the month but regained consciousness briefly. Al-Sarakhsi holds that each day together with its preceding night constitutes the cause; thus, if the person was insane for an entire day-night cycle, no obligation arises. The other view, preferred by the author of al-Hidayah, holds that witnessing any part of the month makes the whole month obligatory, but each day's performance is caused separately. The practical difference (thamarah) appears in qada: according to al-Sarakhsi, if the person recovered only in the first night and then became insane again, no qada is required because the cause (a full day with its night) was not present. According to the other view, qada is required because the person witnessed part of the month. The passage clarifies that al-Hidayah does not see a contradiction between the two statements but notes that its author chooses the latter view.

Key Terms
سبب الوجوب (sabab al-wujub): The cause that makes fasting obligatory; in this passage, disputed whether it is each day with its night or witnessing any part of the month.
إفاقة (ifaqa): Regaining consciousness from insanity or unconsciousness.
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarat al-khilaf): The practical legal consequence of the doctrinal dispute.
قضاء (qada): The makeup of missed obligatory fasts; the ruling disputed here depends on whether the sabab was established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the view preferred by the author of al-Hidayah, if a person recovers consciousness on the first night of Ramadan and then becomes insane before dawn, and remains insane for the entire month, is qada required?
A) Yes, because witnessing part of the month establishes the cause for the whole month.
B) No, because the night is not a valid time for fasting.
C) Yes, but only for the days he was conscious.
D) No, because he did not witness any complete day.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on the sabab of fasting for the insane hinges on whether the cause is each day with its night or witnessing part of the month, with the majority holding that brief consciousness triggers obligation for the entire month.

Purity from Menstruation: Condition for Obligation or Performance?
Internal Hanafi dispute on the classification of purity from menstruation as shart al-wujub versus shart al-ada'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between shart al-wujub and shart al-ada' regarding purity from menstruation in fasting and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
The Hanafi majority holds purity from menstruation is a condition for performance, not obligation; both views require qada'.

This lesson explores an internal Hanafi dispute about the role of purity from menstruation in fasting. The question is whether this purity is a condition for the obligation to fast (shart al-wujub) or for its valid performance (shart al-ada'). While the theoretical classification differs, both views agree that a menstruating woman must make up missed fasts. Understanding this distinction strengthens your grasp of conditions in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The majority (al-'ammah) hold that purity from menstruation is a condition for performance (shart al-ada'). The verifying scholars from Transoxiana hold it is a condition for obligation (shart al-wujub).
2
Examine the majority's proof
The obligation of qada' does not require a prior obligation; it only requires that the worship was missed from its time and that making up is feasible without hardship.
3
Examine the minority's proof
They consider purity a condition for obligation, so the obligation does not arise without it. Qada' is based on the fact that the worship was lost from its time and can be compensated.
4
Analyse the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Both views agree that qada' is required. There is no difference in the final ruling.
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq notes 'perhaps there is no practical consequence'.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The majority view (shart al-ada') is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو كله، وكذا المجنون إذا أفاق في بعض الشهر. وقال بعض أهل التحقق من مشايخ ما وراء النهر : إنه شرط الوجوب . وعندهم لا فرق بينه وبين وجوب الأداء وأجابوا عما استدل به العامة بأن وجوب القضاء لا يستدعي سابقة الوجوب لا محالة وإنما يستدعي فوت العبادة عن وقتها والقدرة على القضاء من غير حرج، وهكذا وقع الاختلاف في الطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس ; فذهب أهل التحقيق إلى أنها شرط الوجوب فلا وجوب على الحائض والنفساء وقضاء الصوم لا يستدعي سابقة الوجوب كما تقدم وعند العامة ليست بشرط وإنما الطهارة عنهما شرط الأداء وتمامه في البدائع ولعله لا ثمرة له
Or all of it, and likewise the insane person if he recovers consciousness during part of the month. And some verifying scholars from the teachers of Transoxiana said: It is a condition for obligation. According to them, there is no difference between it and the obligation of performance. And they responded to what the general scholars argued with: that the obligation of making up does not necessarily require previous obligation, but rather requires only the loss of worship from its time and the ability to make up without hardship. And likewise the difference occurred regarding purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding; so the scholars of verification went that it is a condition for obligation, so there is no obligation on the menstruating woman and postpartum woman, and making up fasting does not require previous obligation as mentioned. And according to the general scholars, it is not a condition, rather purity from them is a condition for performance. And its completion is in al-Bada'i' and perhaps there is no practical consequence.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage presents an internal Hanafi dispute regarding the role of purity from menstruation (and postpartum bleeding) in the obligation of fasting. The issue is whether being pure from menstruation is a condition for the obligation to fast (shart al-wujub) or a condition for the valid performance (shart al-ada'). The mainstream Hanafi position (al-'ammah) holds that it is a condition for performance: a menstruating woman is not required to fast during her period, but she must make up later. The verifying scholars from Transoxiana argued that it is a condition for obligation, meaning that the obligation of fasting does not even apply to a menstruating woman. They responded to the mainstream argument that the obligation of qada' does not require that the obligation was established at the time; it only requires that the worship was missed and the ability to make up exists. Both sides agree that qada' is required. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq mentions that this difference may have no practical consequence (la thamara lahu), but it is significant in usul al-fiqh for understanding the categorization of conditions.

Key Terms
شرط الوجوب (shart al-wujub): A condition that must be present for the obligation itself to arise; if absent, the act is not obligatory at all.
شرط الأداء (shart al-ada'): A condition that must be present for the valid performance of an obligatory act; the obligation exists but cannot be fulfilled without the condition.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up a missed obligatory act after its designated time has passed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, purity from menstruation is a condition for what aspect of fasting?
A) Obligation (wujub)
B) Performance (ada')
C) Validity (sihha)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Purity from menstruation is a condition for performing the fast, not for its obligation; both views require qada'.

Conditions and Categories of Fasting in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines conditions and categories of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid fasting (shurut al-sihha) and classify fasting into its seven legal categories, identifying the ruling for each.

Core Idea
Fasting's validity depends on intention, Islam, and purity; its obligation varies from farḍ to makrūh taḥrīmī.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the conditions that make fasting valid and then lays out the seven-level classification of fasting in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for correctly applying rulings to different types of fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify conditions for performance and validity
The text distinguishes between shart al-ada' (conditions for performance: health and residence) and shart al-sihha (conditions for validity: Islam, purity from menstruation/postpartum, intention). Note that sanity, consciousness, and puberty are not conditions for validity.
Fath al-Qadir excludes intention for disbelievers as they lack valid intention.
2
List the seven categories of fasting
The passage categorizes fasting into farḍ, wājib, sunnah, mandūb, nafl, makrūh tanzīhī, and makrūh taḥrīmī. Each category is defined by the level of obligation or recommendation.
Sunnah here refers to emphasized sunnah (like Ashura with the 9th).
3
Examine the dispute on vowed fasts
The text discusses whether vowed fasts (nadhr) are farḍ or wājib. The preferred view is farḍ due to ijma' on binding nature, but some scholars consider them wājib because denial does not cause kufr.
The thamarah al-khilaf: difference in strictness and permissibility of performing after 'Asr.
4
Understand the correction on Eid fasting
Al-Bada'i' states fasting on Eids is makrūh tanzīhī, but the author refutes this, asserting it is makrūh taḥrīmī (prohibitively disliked) because breaking the fast is obligatory.
Al-Majma' explicitly states haram.
5
Distinguish mustaḥabb, mandūb, and nafl
The passage provides definitions: mustaḥabb is what the Prophet did once and left another time; mandūb is what he did once or twice to teach permissibility; nafl is a broad term for voluntary worship, but in al-Fatḥ it contrasts with sunnah and mandūb.
The uṣūliyyūn's definition is preferred.

Source Text

والنوع الثاني من الشرائط شرط الأداء وجوب وهو الصحة والإقامة، والثالث شرط صحته وهو الإسلام والطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس والنية. كذا في البدائع. واقتصر في فتح القدير على ما عدا الأول لأن الكافر لا نية له في دار الحرب ولم يعلم بفرضية رمضان ثم علم ليس عليه قضاء ما مضى. وزاد في النهاية على شرائط الصحة الوقت القابل ليخرج الليل. وفيه بحث لأن التعليق بالنهار داخل في مفهوم الصوم لا قيد له ولهذا كان التحقيق والكفارات، والواجب المنذور، والمسنون عاشوراء مع التاسع، والمندوب من كل ثلاثة أيام ويندوب فيها الأيام البيض وكل صوم ثبت بالسنة طلبه صوم عاشوراء ونحو صوم داود عليه الصلاة والسلام وعلى سائر الأنبياء، والنفل ما سوى ذلك مما لم تثبت كراهته والمكروه تنزيهاً عاشوراء مفرداً عن التاسع ونحو يوم المهرجان، وتحريماً أيام التشريق والعيدين. كذا في فتح القدير واستثنى في عمدة الفتاوى من كراهة صوم يوم النيروز والمهرجان أن يصوم يوماً قبله فلا يكره كما في يوم الشك، والأظهر أن يضم المنذور بقسميه إلى المفروض كما اختاره في البدائع والمجمع ورجحه في فتح القدير للإجماع على لزومه، وأن يجعل قسم الواجب صوم التطوع بعد الشروع فيه وصوم قضائه عند الإفساد ، كذا في البدائع أيضاً. وبما ذكره المحقق اندفع ما في البدائع من قوله وعندنا يكره الصوم في يومي العيد وأيام التشريق والمستحب هو الإفطار فإنه يفيد أن الصوم فيها مكروه تنزيهاً وليس بصحيح لأن الإفطار واجب متحتم ولهذا صرح في المجمع بحرمة الصوم فيها، وينبغي أن يكون كل صوم رغب فيه الشارع صلى الله عليه وسلم بخصوصه يكون مستحباً وما سواه يكون مندوباً مما لم تثبت كراهيته لا نفلاً لأن الشارع قد رغب في مطلق الصوم فترتب على فعله الثواب بخلاف النفلية المقابلة للندبية فإن ظاهره يقتضي عدم الثواب. وصوم ثم قال : ومنه ما في شرح النقاية أن المستحب ما فعله النبي مرة وتركه أخرى، والمندوب ما فعله مرة أو مرتين تعليماً للجواز. كذا في شرح النقاية. قال المؤلف في كتاب الطهارة ويرد عليه ما رغب فيه ولم يفعله وما جعله تعريفاً للمستحب جعله في المحيط تعريفاً للمندوب فالأولى ما عليه الأصوليون | ثم النفل في اللغة الزيادة، وفي الشريعة زيادة عبادة شرعت لنا لا علينا فيشمل الأقسام الثلاثة، ولذا ترجم المصنف بقوله «باب الوتر والنوافل لكن المراد بالنفل في كلام الفتح ما قابل المسنون والمندوب وظاهره أن المراد به ما رادف
The second type of conditions is the condition for performance and obligation, which is health and residence. The third is the condition for its validity: Islam, purity from menstruation/postpartum, and intention. This is in al-Bada'i'. Fath al-Qadir limited it to excluding the first because the disbeliever has no intention, so it is excluded by conditioning it. They did not make sanity and consciousness conditions for validity, because if someone intends to fast at night then becomes insane during the day or faints, his fast that day is valid. It is only invalid the next day due to lack of intention, because intention from the insane and unconscious is not conceivable, not due to lack of capacity for performance. Puberty is not a condition for validity because the fast of a sane child is valid and he is rewarded. This is in al-Bada'i'. Fath al-Qadir added knowledge of obligation or being in dar al-Islam because if a war captive becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and does not know about the obligation of Ramadan, then learns, he does not have to make up the past. Al-Nihayah added to the conditions of validity: the time being suitable (to exclude the night). There is discussion about this because the connection to daytime is part of the concept of fasting, not a restriction; therefore the verification in usul is that qada', absolute nadhr, and kaffarah fast are unrestricted by time, not time-restricted, as held by Fakhr al-Islam. Its ruling is the discharge of obligation and attainment of reward if it is a binding fast, otherwise the second (reward). This is in Fath al-Qadir and there is discussion, because fasts on prohibited days have no reward, so it is better to say: otherwise the second if it is not prohibited, otherwise validity only. Its divisions are farḍ, wājib, sunnah, mandūb, nafl, and makrūh tanzīhan and taḥrīman. The first (farḍ) is Ramadan, its qada', and expiations. Wājib is the vowed fast. Sunnah is Ashura with the ninth. Mandūb is three days of every month, preferably the white days, and every fast requested by the Sunnah like Ashura and the fast of Dawud. Nafl is whatever is not that and whose dislike is not established. Makrūh tanzihan is Ashura alone without the ninth and similar days like Mihrajan. Makrūh taḥrīman is the days of Tashriq and the two Eids. This is in Fath al-Qadir. In 'Umdat al-Fatawa, an exception is made for the dislike of fasting on Nayruz and Mihrajan: if one fasts a day before, it is not disliked, as on the day of doubt. The more apparent view is to include the vowed fast in both its types under the obligatory (farḍ) as chosen in al-Bada'i' and al-Majma' and preferred in Fath al-Qadir due to consensus (ijma) on its binding nature. And to make the wājib category include the voluntary fast after commencement and its qada' when invalidated, as in al-Bada'i' also. With what the muhaqqiq mentioned, what is in al-Bada'i' is refuted: his saying 'according to us, fasting on the two Eids and days of Tashriq is disliked (makrūh tanzihan) and the recommended is to break the fast' — this implies that fasting them is disliked tanzihan, which is not correct because breaking the fast is absolutely obligatory; hence al-Majma' explicitly states the prohibition of fasting on those days. It is appropriate that every fast which the Lawgiver specifically encouraged should be mustaḥabb, and whatever else is not established as disliked should be mandūb, not nafl, because the Lawgiver encouraged absolute fasting, so doing it entails reward, unlike nafl in contrast to mandūb, whose import suggests no reward. Then he said: Among it is what is in Sharḥ al-Niqayah: al-mustaḥabb is what the Prophet did once and left another time; al-mandūb is what he did once or twice to teach permissibility. This is in Sharḥ al-Niqayah. The author said in the book of purification: It is objected to what is encouraged but not done, and what he made a definition of mustaḥabb in al-Muḥīṭ is a definition of mandūb; so the first (al-Ṣūliyyūn's view) is better. Then nafl linguistically means increase, and in Sharia it is an increase of worship legislated for us, not upon us, so it includes the three categories. Hence the author titled 'Chapter on Witr and Nawafil', but the intended meaning of nafl in the words of al-Fatḥ is what contrasts sunnah and mandūb, and apparent is that what is meant by it is what is synonymous (unfinished).
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the conditions for fasting and its legal categories. It begins by listing the conditions for valid performance (shart al-ada'): health and residence (i.e., not sick or travelling). The conditions for validity (shart al-sihha) are: Islam, purity from menstruation/postpartum, and intention. The commentator notes that Fath al-Qadir excludes the first (intention) for disbelievers because they lack valid intention. Sanity and consciousness are not conditions for validity – if one intends at night then becomes insane or faints during the day, the fast remains valid for that day; the next day fails due to lack of intention. Puberty is also not a condition; a sane child's fast is valid and rewarded. Additional conditions from Fath al-Qadir include knowledge of the obligation or being in dar al-Islam (for a convert in dar al-harb who later learns of Ramadan, no qada' is required). Al-Nihayah adds that the time must be suitable (daytime), but the author criticizes this as redundant since 'connection to daytime' is inherent in the definition of fasting. The passage then classifies fasting into seven categories: farḍ (Ramadan, its makeup, expiations), wājib (vowed fast), sunnah (Ashura with the 9th), mandūb (three days a month, preferably white days, and every fast encouraged by Sunnah like Dawud's fast), nafl (anything else not disliked), makrūh tanzīhī (Ashura alone, days like Mihrajan), and makrūh taḥrīmī (days of Tashriq and Eids). A significant discussion follows on whether vowed fasts are farḍ or wājib. The preferred view is to include vowed fasts under farḍ due to consensus (ijma' al-ummah). However, some scholars question using ijma for this because denial of farḍiyya would not make one a kafir, unlike true farḍ. The author also refutes a statement from al-Bada'i' that fasting on Eids is makrūh tanzīhī; it is actually haram because breaking the fast is obligatory. The passage ends with definitions of mustaḥabb, mandūb, and nafl from various sources, noting terminological differences. Ultimately, nafl is a broad term that includes all supererogatory acts, but in al-Fatḥ it is used specifically for what is not sunnah or mandūb.

Key Terms
فرض (farḍ): An obligation established by decisive evidence (Quran, mutawatir sunnah, ijma'); denial causes kufr.
واجب (wājib): An obligation established by probable evidence; denial does not cause kufr.
مسنون (masnūn): What the Prophet consistently did; this term is used for emphasized sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah).
مندوب (mandūb): An act the Prophet did once or a few times to show permissibility; leaving it is not disliked.
نفل (nafl): A voluntary act of worship; linguistically 'increase'.
مكروه تنزيهاً (makrūh tanzīhan): A disliked act that is closer to permissible; avoiding it is better but doing it is not sinful.
مكروه تحريماً (makrūh taḥrīman): A prohibitively disliked act; nearer to haram; sin to do.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on fasting on the two Eids?
A) Makrūh tanzīhan
B) Makrūh taḥrīman (haram)
C) Mubah
D) Sunnah

Key Takeaway
Fasting is classified into seven categories in Hanafi fiqh, each with distinct rulings; vowed fasts are farḍ, and Eid fasting is haram.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner

Classification of Fasts in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines categories of fasting with examples and addresses intra-Hanafi correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the different types of fasts (fard, wajib, nafl, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi, haram) according to the Hanafi school, and state the ruling for each category.

Core Idea
Fasts are classified into six categories based on evidence strength and legal consequence—knowing each prevents sin and ensures valid worship.

This lesson unpacks the taxonomy of fasts as presented in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The author systematically categorizes fasts from obligatory to prohibited, correcting an earlier view on Eid and Tashriq fasts. Understanding this classification is the foundation for correctly applying the rules of fasting in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the six types mentioned: fard, wajib, nafl, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi, haram. Note that haram is used here synonymously with makruh tahrimi for Eid and Tashriq.
Fard includes Ramadan and nadhr; wajib includes voluntary fasts after commencement and make-up fasts.
2
Locate examples for each
For each category, find the specific fast given as an example: fard (Ramadan, vowed fasts), nafl (any non-disliked fast), makruh tanzihi ('Ashura alone), makruh tahrimi/haram (Eid and Tashriq).
Ashura alone is disliked, but pairing with the 9th removes dislike.
3
Understand the basis
The classification rests on evidence strength: fard requires qat'i evidence, wajib zanni evidence. Nafl is not specifically encouraged (contrast with mustahabb and mandub). Makruh tanzihi is disliked without sin; makruh tahrimi is prohibited-close to haram.
The author argues that Eid/Tashriq are haram, not merely makruh tanzihi, because breaking the fast is obligatory.
4
Recognize the correction of al-Bada'i'
The passage disputes a statement in al-Bada'i' that called Eid/Tashriq fasts makruh tanzihi. The author cites al-Majma' for prohibition, noting that if breaking the fast is obligatory, then fasting is haram.
This shows the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh: later scholars (muhaqqiqun) refine earlier positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم والوعد عليه كصوم داود عليه الصلاة والسلام وعلى سائر الأنبياء، والنفل ما سوى ذلك مما لم يثبت كراهته والمكروه تنزيهاً عاشوراء مفرداً عن التاسع ونحو يوم المهرجان، وتحريماً أيام التشريق والعيدين. كذا في فتح القدير واستثنى في عمدة الفتاوى من كراهة صوم يوم النيروز والمهرجان أن يصوم يوماً قبله فلا يكره كما في يوم الشك، والأظهر أن يضم المنذور بقسميه إلى المفروض كما اختاره في البدائع والمجمع ورجحه في فتح القدير للإجماع على لزومه، وأن يجعل قسم الواجب صوم التطوع بعد الشروع فيه وصوم قضائه عند الإفساد ، كذا في البدائع أيضاً. وبما ذكره المحقق اندفع ما في البدائع من قوله وعندنا يكره الصوم في يومي العيد وأيام التشريق والمستحب هو الإفطار فإنه يفيد أن الصوم فيها مكروه تنزيهاً وليس بصحيح لأن الإفطار واجب متحتم ولهذا صرح في المجمع بحرمة الصوم فيها، وينبغي أن يكون كل صوم رغب فيه الشارع صلى الله عليه وسلم بخصوصه يكون مستحباً وما سواه يكون مندوباً مما لم تثبت كراهيته لا نفلاً لأن الشارع قد رغب في مطلق الصوم فترتب على فعله الثواب بخلاف النفلية المقابلة للندبية فإن ظاهره يقتضي عدم الثواب وصوم اعتكاف . کونه التخصيص، قيل وفيه أي التخصيص نظر إذ من شرطه المقارنة والمخصص غير معلوم فضلاً. عن مقارناً. وأيضاً قوله تعالى فمن شهد منكم الشهر فليصمه ) [البقرة : ١٨٥] خص منه المجانين والصبيان ولم ينتف عنه إثبات الفرضية. وعليه فلا حاجة للإجماع على أنه ممنوع بدليل أن جاحده لا يكفر وقد قال في أوائل السير من المحيط البرهاني والذخيرة : الفرق بين الفرض والواجب ظاهر نظراً إلى الأحكام حتى إن الصلاة المنذورة لا تؤدى بعد صلاة العصر وتقضى الفوائت بعد صلاة العصر ولو كان ثمة إجماع لكانت تؤدى بعده. قال بعض المتأخرين والحق أن التخصيص ثابت بالإجماع يعني على عدم صحة النذر بالمعصية ونحوها، ولا بد من مستند وهو المخصص في الحقيقة والإجماع كاشف عنه ومقرر له وعند عدم العلم بالتاريخ يحمل على المقارنة كما تقرر، ولم ينعقد الإجماع على فرضية ما بقي بعد التخصيص بخلاف آية الصيام ا.هـ.
Book of Fasting. The promised (fast) like the fast of Dawud, peace be upon him and upon all prophets. And nafl is other than that for which dislike is not established. And disliked in the sense of tanzih: 'Ashura singly without the ninth and similar to the day of Mihrajan. And prohibited: the days of Tashriq and the two Eids. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And an exception was made in 'Umdat al-Fatawa from the dislike of fasting on Nayruz and Mihrajan: if one fasts a day before it, it is not disliked, as on the day of doubt. And the more apparent is that the vowed fast (nadhr) in its two divisions is included with the obligatory, as chosen in al-Bada'i' and al-Majma' and preponderated in Fath al-Qadir due to consensus on its binding nature. And that the division of wajib is made: the voluntary fast after commencing it and the fast of its make-up when invalidated, thus in al-Bada'i' also. And by what the investigator mentioned, what is in al-Bada'i' is repelled from his statement: 'According to us, fasting is disliked on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq, and the recommendable is breaking the fast,' for it indicates that fasting therein is disliked as tanzih, and it is not correct because breaking the fast is necessarily obligatory, and for this reason he explicitly stated in al-Majma' the prohibition of fasting therein. And it should be that every fast in which the Lawgiver, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, encouraged specifically is mustahabb, and what is other than that is mandub, from which dislike is not established, not nafl, because the Lawgiver encouraged absolute fasting, so reward is consequent upon its performance, contrary to nafl in contrast to mandub, for its apparent implication is absence of reward. And fasting of i'tikaf. It being takhsiss. It was said, and in it, i.e., the takhsiss, there is consideration, for one of its conditions is conjunction and the specificer is unknown, let alone being conjunct. Also His statement, the Exalted: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (2:185) has been specified to exclude the insane and children, but the affirmation of obligation is not negated from it. Thus there is no need for consensus, for it is prevented by the evidence that its denier does not become an unbeliever. And it was said in the beginning of al-Siyar from al-Muhit al-Burhani and al-Dhakhira: The difference between fard and wajib is clear with regard to rulings, such that the vowed prayer is not performed after Asr prayer, whereas missed prayers are made up after Asr prayer; and if there was consensus, they would be performed after it. Some later scholars said: The truth is that takhsiss is established by consensus, meaning regarding the invalidity of vowing an act of disobedience and the like. And there must be a basis, which is the specificer in reality, and consensus reveals it and confirms it. And when the date is unknown, it is treated as conjunct, as established. And consensus has not been formed on the obligation of what remains after takhsiss, contrary to the verse of fasting. End.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq begins the Book of Fasting by defining the types of fasts. It states that a promised (nadhr) fast is like the fast of Dawud, i.e., obligatory. Nafl is anything not proven disliked. Makruh tanzihi includes fasting on 'Ashura alone without the ninth, and similar days like Mihrajan. Makruh tahrimi includes the days of Tashriq and the two Eids. An exception is made for Nayruz and Mihrajan: if one fasts the day before, it is not disliked, analogous to the day of doubt. The text then says the more apparent view is that nadhr fasts in both types (mu'ayyan and mutlaq) are included in the obligatory category. It also classifies wajib as voluntary fasts after commencement and make-up fasts when invalidated. The author then critiques a statement in al-Bada'i' that claimed fasting on Eids and Tashriq is makruh tanzihi, pointing out that breaking the fast is actually obligatory, so the fast is haram (prohibited). He then distinguishes between mustahabb (fasts specifically encouraged) and mandub (other non-disliked fasts). The discussion shifts to i'tikaf and takhsiss (specification) of the obligation of Ramadan, arguing that the verse is general but specified by consensus for certain exemptions. A debate on fard vs wajib follows, with the conclusion that the difference is clear in rulings such as the permissibility of praying vowed prayers after Asr. The passage is dense and covers multiple foundational issues.

Key Terms
فَرْض (fard): Obligatory fasts like Ramadan and vowed fasts (nadhr) that are binding.
وَاجِب (wajib): Fasts that become obligatory after commencement (voluntary fasts) and make-up fasts when invalidated.
نَفْل (nafl): Voluntary fasts not specifically encouraged, as opposed to mustahabb and mandub.
مَكْرُوه تَنْزِيْهًا (makruh tanzihan): Disliked but not sinful, like fasting on 'Ashura alone.
مَكْرُوه تَحْرِيْمًا (makruh tahriman): Prohibited-disliked, close to haram, like fasting on Eid days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is makruh tanzihi
B) It is makruh tahrimi (prohibited)
C) It is mustahabb
D) It is mubah

Key Takeaway
Proper classification of fasts ensures one knows when fasting is obligatory, recommended, disliked, or prohibited.

The Distinction Between Fard and Wajib in Hanafi Fiqh
Discusses the fard-wajib distinction with evidence from post-Asr prayers and the role of ijma and takhsiss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib, citing the evidence from the ruling on post-Asr prayers and the role of ijma in specifying obligations.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, fard and wajib are distinct categories: fard is proven by qat'i evidence and requires belief, while wajib is proven by zanni evidence and does not require the same level of certainty.

This lesson explores the Hanafi school's unique differentiation between fard and wajib, focusing on the practical example of performing vowed prayers after Asr. The passage uses this example to argue against the view that all obligations are equal, and discusses the role of ijma and takhsiss in establishing the obligation of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on fard vs wajib
Fard is established by qat'i (certain) evidence (e.g., Quran, mutawatir hadith). Wajib is established by zanni (speculative) evidence (e.g., ahad hadith). Denying fard leads to disbelief; denying wajib does not.
This distinction is central to Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
2
Examine the key practical example
The passage states: a vowed (wajib) prayer is not performed after Asr, but missed fard prayers can be made up after Asr. This shows that fard prayers override the prohibition of prayer after Asr, while wajib prayers do not.
The prohibition of prayer after Asr is a zanni-based prohibition, so it can be overridden for fard (qat'i) but not for wajib (zanni).
3
Understand the argument against consensus on obligation
The author argues that if consensus (ijma) had established the obligation of the remaining parts of the fasting verse after takhsiss, then the vowed prayer would also be performed after Asr. Since it is not, this shows that the obligation of fasting (after excluding the insane and children) is fard, not established by ijma alone.
This is a nuanced usul point about the interaction between nass and ijma.
4
Trace the usul principles
The passage uses the example to demonstrate that fard and wajib differ in authority. The verse of fasting (2:185) is general, but specification (takhsiss) for the insane and children does not change the fard nature for others. Deniers of wajib do not become kafir, indicating it is not fard.
Takhsiss is established by consensus, but that consensus does not create fard; the original text does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم والوعد عليه كصوم داود عليه الصلاة والسلام وعلى سائر الأنبياء، والنفل ما سوى ذلك مما لم يثبت كراهته والمكروه تنزيهاً عاشوراء مفرداً عن التاسع ونحو يوم المهرجان، وتحريماً أيام التشريق والعيدين. كذا في فتح القدير واستثنى في عمدة الفتاوى من كراهة صوم يوم النيروز والمهرجان أن يصوم يوماً قبله فلا يكره كما في يوم الشك، والأظهر أن يضم المنذور بقسميه إلى المفروض كما اختاره في البدائع والمجمع ورجحه في فتح القدير للإجماع على لزومه، وأن يجعل قسم الواجب صوم التطوع بعد الشروع فيه وصوم قضائه عند الإفساد ، كذا في البدائع أيضاً. وبما ذكره المحقق اندفع ما في البدائع من قوله وعندنا يكره الصوم في يومي العيد وأيام التشريق والمستحب هو الإفطار فإنه يفيد أن الصوم فيها مكروه تنزيهاً وليس بصحيح لأن الإفطار واجب متحتم ولهذا صرح في المجمع بحرمة الصوم فيها، وينبغي أن يكون كل صوم رغب فيه الشارع صلى الله عليه وسلم بخصوصه يكون مستحباً وما سواه يكون مندوباً مما لم تثبت كراهيته لا نفلاً لأن الشارع قد رغب في مطلق الصوم فترتب على فعله الثواب بخلاف النفلية المقابلة للندبية فإن ظاهره يقتضي عدم الثواب وصوم اعتكاف . کونه التخصيص، قيل وفيه أي التخصيص نظر إذ من شرطه المقارنة والمخصص غير معلوم فضلاً. عن مقارناً. وأيضاً قوله تعالى فمن شهد منكم الشهر فليصمه ) [البقرة : ١٨٥] خص منه المجانين والصبيان ولم ينتف عنه إثبات الفرضية. وعليه فلا حاجة للإجماع على أنه ممنوع بدليل أن جاحده لا يكفر وقد قال في أوائل السير من المحيط البرهاني والذخيرة : الفرق بين الفرض والواجب ظاهر نظراً إلى الأحكام حتى إن الصلاة المنذورة لا تؤدى بعد صلاة العصر وتقضى الفوائت بعد صلاة العصر ولو كان ثمة إجماع لكانت تؤدى بعده. قال بعض المتأخرين والحق أن التخصيص ثابت بالإجماع يعني على عدم صحة النذر بالمعصية ونحوها، ولا بد من مستند وهو المخصص في الحقيقة والإجماع كاشف عنه ومقرر له وعند عدم العلم بالتاريخ يحمل على المقارنة كما تقرر، ولم ينعقد الإجماع على فرضية ما بقي بعد التخصيص بخلاف آية الصيام ا.هـ.
Book of Fasting. The promised (fast) like the fast of Dawud, peace be upon him and upon all prophets. And nafl is other than that for which dislike is not established. And disliked in the sense of tanzih: 'Ashura singly without the ninth and similar to the day of Mihrajan. And prohibited: the days of Tashriq and the two Eids. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And an exception was made in 'Umdat al-Fatawa from the dislike of fasting on Nayruz and Mihrajan: if one fasts a day before it, it is not disliked, as on the day of doubt. And the more apparent is that the vowed fast (nadhr) in its two divisions is included with the obligatory, as chosen in al-Bada'i' and al-Majma' and preponderated in Fath al-Qadir due to consensus on its binding nature. And that the division of wajib is made: the voluntary fast after commencing it and the fast of its make-up when invalidated, thus in al-Bada'i' also. And by what the investigator mentioned, what is in al-Bada'i' is repelled from his statement: 'According to us, fasting is disliked on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq, and the recommendable is breaking the fast,' for it indicates that fasting therein is disliked as tanzih, and it is not correct because breaking the fast is necessarily obligatory, and for this reason he explicitly stated in al-Majma' the prohibition of fasting therein. And it should be that every fast in which the Lawgiver, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, encouraged specifically is mustahabb, and what is other than that is mandub, from which dislike is not established, not nafl, because the Lawgiver encouraged absolute fasting, so reward is consequent upon its performance, contrary to nafl in contrast to mandub, for its apparent implication is absence of reward. And fasting of i'tikaf. It being takhsiss. It was said, and in it, i.e., the takhsiss, there is consideration, for one of its conditions is conjunction and the specificer is unknown, let alone being conjunct. Also His statement, the Exalted: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (2:185) has been specified to exclude the insane and children, but the affirmation of obligation is not negated from it. Thus there is no need for consensus, for it is prevented by the evidence that its denier does not become an unbeliever. And it was said in the beginning of al-Siyar from al-Muhit al-Burhani and al-Dhakhira: The difference between fard and wajib is clear with regard to rulings, such that the vowed prayer is not performed after Asr prayer, whereas missed prayers are made up after Asr prayer; and if there was consensus, they would be performed after it. Some later scholars said: The truth is that takhsiss is established by consensus, meaning regarding the invalidity of vowing an act of disobedience and the like. And there must be a basis, which is the specificer in reality, and consensus reveals it and confirms it. And when the date is unknown, it is treated as conjunct, as established. And consensus has not been formed on the obligation of what remains after takhsiss, contrary to the verse of fasting. End.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq begins the Book of Fasting by defining the types of fasts. It states that a promised (nadhr) fast is like the fast of Dawud, i.e., obligatory. Nafl is anything not proven disliked. Makruh tanzihi includes fasting on 'Ashura alone without the ninth, and similar days like Mihrajan. Makruh tahrimi includes the days of Tashriq and the two Eids. An exception is made for Nayruz and Mihrajan: if one fasts the day before, it is not disliked, analogous to the day of doubt. The text then says the more apparent view is that nadhr fasts in both types (mu'ayyan and mutlaq) are included in the obligatory category. It also classifies wajib as voluntary fasts after commencement and make-up fasts when invalidated. The author then critiques a statement in al-Bada'i' that claimed fasting on Eids and Tashriq is makruh tanzihi, pointing out that breaking the fast is actually obligatory, so the fast is haram (prohibited). He then distinguishes between mustahabb (fasts specifically encouraged) and mandub (other non-disliked fasts). The discussion shifts to i'tikaf and takhsiss (specification) of the obligation of Ramadan, arguing that the verse is general but specified by consensus for certain exemptions. A debate on fard vs wajib follows, with the conclusion that the difference is clear in rulings such as the permissibility of praying vowed prayers after Asr. The passage is dense and covers multiple foundational issues.

Key Terms
فَرْض (fard): Obligatory act proven by qat'i (certain) evidence; denial leads to kufr.
وَاجِب (wajib): Obligatory act proven by zanni (speculative) evidence; denial does not lead to kufr.
إِجْمَاع (ijma): Consensus of scholars; in this passage, used to establish takhsiss and the invalidity of vowing disobedience.
تَخْصِيص (takhsiss): Specification of a general text; here, the verse of fasting is specified to exclude the insane and children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical difference between fard and wajib prayers?
A) Fard prayers can be made up after Asr, but wajib prayers cannot
B) Wajib prayers can be made up after Asr, but fard prayers cannot
C) Both can be made up after Asr
D) Neither can be made up after Asr

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes fard from wajib based on the certainty of evidence, affecting many practical rulings.

Disliked Fasts in the Hanafi School
Lists disliked fasts with reasons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fasts that are makruh in the Hanafi school and state the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Not all voluntary fasts are recommended; some are disliked due to resemblance to non-Muslims or potential harm.

The Hanafi school catalogs several fasts that are disliked (makruh). These include fasting on the day of doubt, continuous fasting (wisal), fasting on Saturday alone, and fasting in silence. Understanding why each is disliked helps us avoid practices that may be spiritually or physically detrimental. Let's explore each one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the day-of-doubt fast
Find the mention of 'صوم يوم الشك' and note that it is makruh due to uncertainty about whether it is Ramadan or not.
Details are promised later in the text.
2
Understand wisal (continuous fasting)
Find the definition of wisal: fasting two days without breaking at night. It is makruh.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad defined it.
3
Distinguish Saturday from Friday fasting
Fasting Saturday alone is makruh due to resembling Jews, while fasting Friday alone is mustahabb.
This shows the legal reasoning based on resemblance to non-Muslims.
4
Analyze the Shawwal fasts dispute
Abu Hanifa allows six days of Shawwal (separate or consecutive). Abu Yusuf dislikes consecutive. Later scholars see no harm.
The majority view (mufta bihi) is permissibility.
5
Note the silence fast
Fasting while refraining from speech as well is makruh.
This is mentioned in al-Bada'i'.

فيه، وكل صوم يؤمر فيه بالتتابع لأجل أن الوقت مفوت ذلك يسقط التتابع، وإن بقي الفعل واجب القضاء فالأول كصوم كفارة القتل والظهار واليمين والإفطار ويلحق به النذر المطلق إذا ذكر التتابع فيه أو نواه، والثاني كرمضان والنذر المعين واليمين معين . بصوم يوم كذا ذكره صاحب البدائع والأسبيجابي مختصراً، ومحاسنه كثيرة منها شكر النعمة اتي هي المفطرات الثلاثة لأن بضدها تتبين الأشياء، ومنها أنه وسيلة إلى التقوى لأنها إذا انقادت إلى الامتناع عن الحلال طمعاً في مرضاته تعالى فالأولى أن تنقاد للامتناع عن الحرام وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى لعلكم تتقون ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸۳] ومنها كسر الشهوة الداعية إلى المعاصي، التفرقة بين المستحب والمندوب، وبيان أن المراد بالنفل في كلامه المندوب لئلا يـ يرد عليه المحذور . هذا ما ظهر لي والله تعالى أعلم قوله : (على ما سنذكره أي من التفصيل الآتي عند قوله ولا يصام يوم الشك إلا The day of fasting therein and otherwise it is mandub, as is not hidden. And from the disliked is fasting on the day of doubt, as we will mention, Allah willing. And from it is the fast of wisal (continuous fasting), which Abu Yusuf and Muhammad explained as fasting two days without breaking the fast between them. And from it is the fast of the day of 'Arafa for the pilgrim if it weakens him. And from it is fasting Saturday alone due to resemblance to Jews, unlike fasting Friday, for fasting it alone is mustahabb according to the general public, like Monday and Thursday, and some disliked all of it. And from it is silence (samt), i.e., refraining from food and speech altogether. Thus in al-Bada'i'. And from it also is fasting six days of Shawwal according to Abu Hanifa, whether separate or consecutive, and from Abu Yusuf its dislike if consecutive, not separate, but the majority of later scholars saw no harm in it. Then know that the necessary fasts as fard are thirteen: seven of them require continuity (tataabu') – these are: Ramadan, expiation for homicide, expiation for zihar, expiation for an oath, expiation for breaking the fast in Ramadan, the specified vowed fast, and the specified oath fast. And six do not require continuity: make-up of Ramadan, tamattu' fast, expiation for shaving (the head), penalty for hunting, the absolute vowed fast, and the oath fast where one says 'By Allah, I will fast a month'. Then, if one breaks a fast that requires continuity, does he have to start over or not? We say: Every fast that is commanded with continuity for the sake of the act – i.e., fasting itself – continuity is a condition in it. And every fast that is commanded with continuity because the time is lost – continuity is waived, though the act remains obligatory to make up. The first type includes expiation for homicide, zihar, oath, and breaking the fast, and the absolute vowed fast joins it if continuity is mentioned or intended. The second type includes Ramadan, the specified vowed fast, and the specified oath fast for a specific day. This is what the author of al-Bada'i' and al-Isbijabi mentioned briefly. And its benefits (of fasting) are many: among them is gratitude for the blessings which are the three breakers (of fast) because things are known by their opposites; among them is that it is a means to taqwa because if the soul submits to abstaining from the lawful hoping for Allah's pleasure, it is more likely to submit to abstaining from the unlawful, and to this refers His statement, Exalted: 'that you may become righteous' (2:183); among them is breaking the desire that leads to sins; among them is being characterized with the attribute of spiritual angels; among them is knowing the state of the poor to have mercy on them and feed them; among them is conformity with them. The permissible (mubah) is that which has no reward, and there is no doubt that every fast that is not disliked or prohibited has reward; therefore the author was compelled to distinguish between mustahabb and mandub, and to clarify that the intended meaning of nafl in his speech is mandub, lest the objection occur. This is what appeared to me, and Allah knows best. His statement: 'as we will mention' i.e., the detail to come at his statement: 'and the day of doubt is not fasted except as voluntary'. His statement: 'and from it is fasting Saturday alone, and likewise Sunday'. He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: Fasting on Nayruz and Mihrajan is disliked if one intentionally does it and it does not coincide with a day he used to fast before. And similarly it was said about Saturday and Sunday. His statement: 'but the majority of later scholars saw no harm in it' – Allamah Qasim quoted their expressions in his Fatawa and refuted the one who declared the dislike correct, so consult it. And in al-Fath after what passed: They differed, for it was said the best is to connect it (six days of Shawwal) with the day of Fitr, and it was said rather to separate them in the month. His statement: 'continuity is a condition in it' i.e., if breaking the fast occurs in its midst, one must start over. His statement: 'continuity is waived' i.e., if he breaks the fast in its midst, he does not start over but builds on what passed.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists the fasts that are disliked (makruh) and then provides a detailed classification of the thirteen necessary fasts (اللازمة فرضاً). The disliked fasts include: the day of doubt (30th of Sha'ban), wisal (continuous fasting for two days without breaking), 'Arafa for a pilgrim if it weakens him, fasting Saturday alone due to resemblance to Jews (unlike Friday which is mustahabb), fasting in silence (refraining from both food and speech), and six days of Shawwal (with a difference of opinion: Abu Hanifa allows it, Abu Yusuf dislikes consecutive, but later scholars see no problem). Then the passage explains that there are thirteen necessary fasts (fard): seven require continuous fasting (tataabu'), meaning if broken, one must start over. These are: Ramadan, expiation for homicide, expiation for zihar, expiation for an oath, expiation for breaking the fast in Ramadan, a specified vowed fast (nadhru mu'ayyan), and a specified oath fast. Six do not require continuity: make-up of Ramadan, tamattu' fast, expiation for shaving (hajj), penalty for hunting, absolute vowed fast, and an oath fast for a month. The ruling when one breaks a continuous fast: if continuity is required for the act itself (e.g., expiations), then breaking necessitates starting over (istiqbal). If continuity is required because the time is fixed (e.g., Ramadan), then breaking does not require starting over; one simply makes up the missed days. This applies to Ramadan, specified vows, and specified oaths. The passage concludes with the wisdom of fasting: gratitude, taqwa, breaking desires, angelic qualities, and empathy for the poor.

Key Terms
صَوْمُ الْوِصَال (sawm al-wisal): Continuous fasting for two days without breaking the fast at night.
الصَّمْتُ (al-samt): Refraining from food and speech during the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Mustahabb
B) Makruh because it resembles Jews
C) Haram
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school identifies several fasts as makruh to avoid harm or resemblance to non-Muslims, with the ruling on Shawwal consecutive fasting being the subject of a resolved ikhtilaf.

The Thirteen Necessary Fasts and the Rule of Continuity
Classifies necessary fasts by continuity requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the thirteen necessary fasts into those requiring continuity (tataabu') and those that do not, and state the consequence of breaking a continuous fast.

Core Idea
Breaking a fast that requires continuity (tataabu') forces a restart; breaking one where continuity is due to time loss lets you build on what you fasted.

The Hanafi school lists thirteen fasts that are obligatory (fard or wajib). Some require continuous fasting (tataabu'), meaning they must be observed without interruption. Others allow breaks. The key distinction: if continuity is a condition of the act itself, breaking nullifies the sequence; if it is due to the fixed time (like Ramadan), you simply make up the missed days.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thirteen necessary fasts
The passage states they are fard (or wajib). List them: Ramadan, various expiations, vowed fasts, etc.
Some are fard 'ayn, some are wajib.
2
Classify by continuity requirement
Seven require tataabu': Ramadan, expiations for killing, zihar, oath, breaking Ramadan fast, specified vow, specified oath. Six do not: qada of Ramadan, tamattu', shaving, hunting, absolute vow, oath for a month.
Memorize the lists.
3
Understand 'continuity as a condition for the act'
For expiations and similar, continuity (tataabu') is a condition (shart) of the act. Breaking forces restart.
This is because the act itself requires consecutiveness.
4
Understand 'continuity due to time loss'
For Ramadan and specified vowed/oath fasts, continuity is only due to the fixed time. Breaking does not void the whole sequence; you only make up the missed days.
The time has passed, so continuity is waived.
5
Apply the rule to examples
If someone breaks an expiation fast, they must start over. If they break a Ramadan fast (validly), they only make up that day.
This is the practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ومنه صوم يوم السبت بانفراده للتشبه باليهود بخلاف صوم الجمعة فإن صومه بانفراده مستحب عند العامة كالاثنين والخميس وكره الكل بعضهم، ومنه الصمت بأن يمسك عن الطعام والكلام .جميعاً. كذا في البدائع. ومنه أيضاً صوم ستة من شوّال عند أبي حنيفة متفرقاً كان أو متتابعاً، وعن أبي يوسف كراهته متتابعاً لا متفرقاً لكن عامة المتأخرين لم يروا به بأساً. ثم اعلم أن الصيامات اللازمة فرضاً ثلاثة عشر، بسبعة منها يجب فيها التتابع وهي: رمضان وكفارة القتل وكفارة الظهار وكفارة اليمين وكفارة الإفطار في رمضان والنذر المعين وصوم اليمين المعين وسنة لا يجب فيها التتابع وهي : قضاء رمضان وصوم المتعة وصوم كفارة الحلق وصوم جزاء الصيد وصوم النذر المطلق وصوم اليمين بأن قال والله لأصومن شهراً. ثم أذا أفطر يوماً يجب فيه التتابع هل يلزمه الاستقبال أو لا فنقول : كل صوم يؤمر فيه بالتتابع لأجل الفعل وهو الصوم يكون التتابع شرطاً فيه، وكل صوم يؤمر فيه بالتتابع لأجل أن الوقت مفوت ذلك يسقط التتابع، وإن بقي الفعل واجب القضاء فالأول كصوم كفارة القتل والظهار واليمين والإفطار ويلحق به النذر المطلق إذا ذكر التتابع فيه أو نواه، والثاني كرمضان والنذر المعين واليمين معين . بصوم يوم كذا ذكره صاحب البدائع والأسبيجابي مختصراً، ومحاسنه كثيرة منها شكر النعمة اتي هي المفطرات الثلاثة لأن بضدها تتبين الأشياء، ومنها أنه وسيلة إلى التقوى لأنها إذا انقادت إلى الامتناع عن الحلال طمعاً في مرضاته تعالى فالأولى أن تنقاد للامتناع عن الحرام وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى لعلكم تتقون ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸۳] ومنها كسر الشهوة الداعية إلى المعاصي، ومنها الاتصاف بصفة الملائكة الروحانية، ومنها علمه بحال الفقراء ليرحمهم فيطعمهم، ومنها موافقته لهم . المباح مما لا ثواب فيه، ولا شك أن كل صوم لم يكن مكروهاً ولا محرماً يثاب عليه فلذا اضطر المؤلف إلى التفرقة بين المستحب والمندوب، وبيان أن المراد بالنفل في كلامه المندوب لئلا يـ يرد عليه المحذور . هذا ما ظهر لي والله تعالى أعلم قوله : (على ما سنذكره أي من التفصيل الآتي عند قوله ولا يصام يوم الشك إلا تطوعاً قوله : ( ومنه صوم يوم السبت بانفراده وكذا : يوم الأحد. قال في التتارخانية : ويكره صوم النيروز والمهرجان إذا تعمده ولم يوافق يوماً كان يصومه قبل ذلك، وهكذا قيل في يوم السبت الأحد قوله : (لكن عامة المتأخرين لم يروا به بأساً قد سرد عبارتهم العلامة قاسم في فتاواه ورد قول من صحح الكراهة فراجعه . وفي الفتح بعد ما مر : واختلفوا فقيل الأفضل وصلها بيوم الفطر، وقيل بل تفريقها في الشهر قوله : (يكون التتابع شرطاً فيه) أي فإذا تخلل الفطر في خلاله يلزمه الاستقبال قوله : ( يسقط التتابع) أي فلو أفطر في خلاله لا يستقبل بل يبني على ما فات .
The day of fasting therein and otherwise it is mandub, as is not hidden. And from the disliked is fasting on the day of doubt, as we will mention, Allah willing. And from it is the fast of wisal (continuous fasting), which Abu Yusuf and Muhammad explained as fasting two days without breaking the fast between them. And from it is the fast of the day of 'Arafa for the pilgrim if it weakens him. And from it is fasting Saturday alone due to resemblance to Jews, unlike fasting Friday, for fasting it alone is mustahabb according to the general public, like Monday and Thursday, and some disliked all of it. And from it is silence (samt), i.e., refraining from food and speech altogether. Thus in al-Bada'i'. And from it also is fasting six days of Shawwal according to Abu Hanifa, whether separate or consecutive, and from Abu Yusuf its dislike if consecutive, not separate, but the majority of later scholars saw no harm in it. Then know that the necessary fasts as fard are thirteen: seven of them require continuity (tataabu') – these are: Ramadan, expiation for homicide, expiation for zihar, expiation for an oath, expiation for breaking the fast in Ramadan, the specified vowed fast, and the specified oath fast. And six do not require continuity: make-up of Ramadan, tamattu' fast, expiation for shaving (the head), penalty for hunting, the absolute vowed fast, and the oath fast where one says 'By Allah, I will fast a month'. Then, if one breaks a fast that requires continuity, does he have to start over or not? We say: Every fast that is commanded with continuity for the sake of the act – i.e., fasting itself – continuity is a condition in it. And every fast that is commanded with continuity because the time is lost – continuity is waived, though the act remains obligatory to make up. The first type includes expiation for homicide, zihar, oath, and breaking the fast, and the absolute vowed fast joins it if continuity is mentioned or intended. The second type includes Ramadan, the specified vowed fast, and the specified oath fast for a specific day. This is what the author of al-Bada'i' and al-Isbijabi mentioned briefly. And its benefits (of fasting) are many: among them is gratitude for the blessings which are the three breakers (of fast) because things are known by their opposites; among them is that it is a means to taqwa because if the soul submits to abstaining from the lawful hoping for Allah's pleasure, it is more likely to submit to abstaining from the unlawful, and to this refers His statement, Exalted: 'that you may become righteous' (2:183); among them is breaking the desire that leads to sins; among them is being characterized with the attribute of spiritual angels; among them is knowing the state of the poor to have mercy on them and feed them; among them is conformity with them. The permissible (mubah) is that which has no reward, and there is no doubt that every fast that is not disliked or prohibited has reward; therefore the author was compelled to distinguish between mustahabb and mandub, and to clarify that the intended meaning of nafl in his speech is mandub, lest the objection occur. This is what appeared to me, and Allah knows best. His statement: 'as we will mention' i.e., the detail to come at his statement: 'and the day of doubt is not fasted except as voluntary'. His statement: 'and from it is fasting Saturday alone, and likewise Sunday'. He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: Fasting on Nayruz and Mihrajan is disliked if one intentionally does it and it does not coincide with a day he used to fast before. And similarly it was said about Saturday and Sunday. His statement: 'but the majority of later scholars saw no harm in it' – Allamah Qasim quoted their expressions in his Fatawa and refuted the one who declared the dislike correct, so consult it. And in al-Fath after what passed: They differed, for it was said the best is to connect it (six days of Shawwal) with the day of Fitr, and it was said rather to separate them in the month. His statement: 'continuity is a condition in it' i.e., if breaking the fast occurs in its midst, one must start over. His statement: 'continuity is waived' i.e., if he breaks the fast in its midst, he does not start over but builds on what passed.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists the fasts that are disliked (makruh) and then provides a detailed classification of the thirteen necessary fasts (اللازمة فرضاً). The disliked fasts include: the day of doubt (30th of Sha'ban), wisal (continuous fasting for two days without breaking), 'Arafa for a pilgrim if it weakens him, fasting Saturday alone due to resemblance to Jews (unlike Friday which is mustahabb), fasting in silence (refraining from both food and speech), and six days of Shawwal (with a difference of opinion: Abu Hanifa allows it, Abu Yusuf dislikes consecutive, but later scholars see no problem). Then the passage explains that there are thirteen necessary fasts (fard): seven require continuous fasting (tataabu'), meaning if broken, one must start over. These are: Ramadan, expiation for homicide, expiation for zihar, expiation for an oath, expiation for breaking the fast in Ramadan, a specified vowed fast (nadhru mu'ayyan), and a specified oath fast. Six do not require continuity: make-up of Ramadan, tamattu' fast, expiation for shaving (hajj), penalty for hunting, absolute vowed fast, and an oath fast for a month. The ruling when one breaks a continuous fast: if continuity is required for the act itself (e.g., expiations), then breaking necessitates starting over (istiqbal). If continuity is required because the time is fixed (e.g., Ramadan), then breaking does not require starting over; one simply makes up the missed days. This applies to Ramadan, specified vows, and specified oaths. The passage concludes with the wisdom of fasting: gratitude, taqwa, breaking desires, angelic qualities, and empathy for the poor.

Key Terms
التَّتَابُع (al-tataabu'): Continuity of fasting without interruption; a condition for certain fasts.
النَّذْر الْمُعَيَّن (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan): A vowed fast specified to particular days (e.g., 'I will fast Monday').
النَّذْر الْمُطْلَق (al-nadhr al-mutlaq): An absolute vowed fast without specifying days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone breaks a fast during Ramadan, do they need to restart the entire month?
A) Yes, because Ramadan requires continuity
B) No, they only make up the missed day
C) It depends on the intention
D) They must fast two months instead

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh classifies obligatory fasts by whether continuity is a condition of the act or due to fixed time, determining whether breaking forces a restart or simply a make-up.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
