Makruh Fasts in the Hanafi School
Lists various disliked fasts with rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify five types of makruh fasts according to the Hanafi school and state the ruling for each.

Core Idea
Not all fasts are virtuous; fasting on days of doubt, continuous fasting, or imitating other religions is makruh.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists fasts that are considered makruh (disliked) in the Hanafi school. Understanding these exceptions helps a Muslim avoid practices that may reduce reward or contradict prophetic guidance. This lesson examines each type and the reasoning behind its dislike.

Guided Path
1
Identify each makruh fast
List the fasts explicitly mentioned: day of doubt, wisal (continuous), day of 'Arafah for the pilgrim if it weakens him, Saturday alone, and silent fasting. Also note the mention of Nawruz and Mihrajan from the commentary.
Some fasts like Friday alone are excluded from the list as they are mustahabb.
2
Understand the reason for dislike
For each fast, note the basis for karaah: imitation of Jews (Saturday), potential weakness (day of 'Arafah), or deviation from sunnah (wisal).
The reason for day of doubt is the uncertainty about Ramadan.
3
Distinguish between makruh tanzihi and tahrimi
All listed fasts are makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful), except possibly if imitation is intended. The author does not categorize them as tahrimi.
4
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
Conclude that these fasts are valid but disliked. If one has a prior habit (e.g., fasting every Monday), a fast falling on a makruh day may be exempted.
The passage mentions that fasting the day of doubt is permitted if one habitually fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم. صوم فيه وإلا فهو مندوب كما لا يخفى. ومن المكروه صوم يوم الشك على ما سنذكره إن شاء الله تعالى. ومنه صوم الوصال، وقد فسره أبو يوسف ومحمد بصوم يومين لا فطر بينهما. ومنه صوم يوم عرفة للحاج إن أضعفه. ومنه صوم يوم السبت بانفراده للتشبه باليهود بخلاف صوم الجمعة فإن صومه بانفراده مستحب عند العامة كالاثنين والخميس وكره الكل بعضهم. ومنه الصمت بأن يمسك عن الطعام والكلام جميعاً. كذا في البدائع. ومنه أيضاً صوم ستة من شوّال عند أبي حنيفة متفرقاً كان أو متتابعاً، وعن أبي يوسف كراهته متتابعاً لا متفرقاً لكن عامة المتأخرين لم يروا به بأساً. قوله : (على ما سنذكره) أي من التفصيل الآتي عند قوله ولا يصام يوم الشك إلا تطوعاً. قوله : ( ومنه صوم يوم السبت بانفراده وكذا : يوم الأحد. قال في التتارخانية : ويكره صوم النيروز والمهرجان إذا تعمده ولم يوافق يوماً كان يصومه قبل ذلك، وهكذا قيل في يوم السبت الأحد. قوله : (لكن عامة المتأخرين لم يروا به بأساً) قد سرد عبارتهم العلامة قاسم في فتاواه ورد قول من صحح الكراهة فراجعه. وفي الفتح بعد ما مر : واختلفوا فقيل الأفضل وصلها بيوم الفطر، وقيل بل تفريقها في الشهر.
The Book of Fasting. Any fast in which there is [reward] is [recommended], otherwise it is mandoob as is not hidden. Among the makruh fasts is the fast of the day of doubt, as we will mention. And among them is the fast of wisal, which Abu Yusuf and Muhammad interpreted as fasting two days without breaking fast between them. And among them is the fast of the day of 'Arafah for the pilgrim if it weakens him. And among them is fasting Saturday alone due to imitation of Jews, unlike fasting Friday, for fasting it alone is mustahabb according to the general scholars, like Monday and Thursday, but some disliked it. And among them is silence, i.e., refraining from both food and speech, as in al-Bada'i'. And also among them is fasting six days of Shawwal according to Abu Hanifa, whether scattered or consecutive. But from Abu Yusuf there is a narration of its dislike if consecutive, not if scattered. However, the majority of later scholars saw no harm in it. [Commentary] His saying: 'as we will mention' means the detailed discussion later at his saying: 'The day of doubt is not fasted except voluntarily.' His saying: 'And among them is fasting Saturday alone, and similarly Sunday.' It is said in al-Tatarakhaniyya: It is disliked to fast Nawruz and Mihrajan deliberately if it does not coincide with a day one used to fast before, and similarly it is said about Saturday and Sunday. His saying: 'But the majority of later scholars saw no harm in it' – Allama Qasim has cited their statements in his Fatawa and refuted those who affirmed the dislike, so refer to it. And in al-Fath, after what has passed: They differed: some said it is better to join them to the day of Fitr, others said to scatter them throughout the month.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding which fasts are considered makruh (disliked) and which are mustahabb or mandoob. The author begins by stating that any fast that is not specifically characterized is mandoob (recommended). He then enumerates several types of makruh fasts: the fast of the day of doubt (when Ramadan is uncertain), the fast of wisal (continuous fasting for two days without breaking fast at night, as defined by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), fasting on the day of 'Arafah for a pilgrim if it would weaken him, fasting Saturday alone because it resembles the practice of Jews, and the fast of complete silence (refraining from both food and speech). The passage then addresses a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute: the fast of six days of Shawwal. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is permissible (and recommended) whether performed consecutively or separately. However, a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf indicates that it is makruh if done consecutively (i.e., immediately after Eid al-Fitr) but not if scattered. The commentary notes that the majority of later Hanafi scholars considered it permissible and saw no harm in it, citing the Fatawa of Allama Qasim. The passage also mentions that some scholars preferred to join the six days to the day of Fitr, while others preferred to scatter them throughout the month. The overall lesson is that the Hanafi school allows room for ijtihad and preference, and the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that fasting six days of Shawwal is recommended and not makruh, regardless of consecutiveness.

Key Terms
صوم الوصال (sawm al-wisal): Fasting two consecutive days without breaking fast at night; defined by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
يوم الشك (yawm al-shakk): The 30th day of Sha'ban when Ramadan is uncertain.
صوم ستة من شوال (sawm sittah min Shawwal): Fasting six days during the month of Shawwal after Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered makruh according to the passage?
A) Fasting the day of doubt without a habit
B) Fasting two consecutive days without breaking fast at night
C) Fasting Saturday alone
D) Fasting Friday alone

Key Takeaway
Fasting is not always commendable; certain days and methods are disliked and should be avoided to maximize reward.

The Shawwal Six: Resolving the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on consecutiveness of Shawwal fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting six days of Shawwal, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Fasting six days of Shawwal is recommended, and despite a narration of dislike if consecutive, the later Hanafi consensus permits it.

One of the most discussed voluntary fasts is the six days of Shawwal. While Imam Abu Hanifa considered it recommended whether consecutive or scattered, a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf disliked consecutiveness. This lesson examines the dispute and the final relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the basic Hanafi ruling
According to Imam Abu Hanifa (zahir al-riwayah), fasting six days of Shawwal is mustahabb (recommended) whether performed consecutively or scattered. This is the default position in the school.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
A narration from Imam Abu Yusuf states that it is makruh to fast them consecutively, but not if scattered. The reason for this dislike is not explicitly given in the passage.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the madhhab, reflecting different ijtihad.
3
Analyze the tarjih (preference) by later scholars
The majority of later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirin) dismissed the karaah and considered it permissible to fast consecutively. This is attested by 'Allama Qasim in his Fatawa and in al-Fath.
The passage uses the phrase 'عامة المتأخرين لم يروا به بأساً' – the majority saw no harm.
4
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
The operative ruling for fatwa today is that there is no dislike; fasting six days of Shawwal is mustahabb, and one may fast them consecutively or scattered, with no distinction.
Some scholars preferred to join them to Eid (consecutively), others to scatter; both are acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم. صوم فيه وإلا فهو مندوب كما لا يخفى. ومن المكروه صوم يوم الشك على ما سنذكره إن شاء الله تعالى. ومنه صوم الوصال، وقد فسره أبو يوسف ومحمد بصوم يومين لا فطر بينهما. ومنه صوم يوم عرفة للحاج إن أضعفه. ومنه صوم يوم السبت بانفراده للتشبه باليهود بخلاف صوم الجمعة فإن صومه بانفراده مستحب عند العامة كالاثنين والخميس وكره الكل بعضهم. ومنه الصمت بأن يمسك عن الطعام والكلام جميعاً. كذا في البدائع. ومنه أيضاً صوم ستة من شوّال عند أبي حنيفة متفرقاً كان أو متتابعاً، وعن أبي يوسف كراهته متتابعاً لا متفرقاً لكن عامة المتأخرين لم يروا به بأساً. قوله : (على ما سنذكره) أي من التفصيل الآتي عند قوله ولا يصام يوم الشك إلا تطوعاً. قوله : ( ومنه صوم يوم السبت بانفراده وكذا : يوم الأحد. قال في التتارخانية : ويكره صوم النيروز والمهرجان إذا تعمده ولم يوافق يوماً كان يصومه قبل ذلك، وهكذا قيل في يوم السبت الأحد. قوله : (لكن عامة المتأخرين لم يروا به بأساً) قد سرد عبارتهم العلامة قاسم في فتاواه ورد قول من صحح الكراهة فراجعه. وفي الفتح بعد ما مر : واختلفوا فقيل الأفضل وصلها بيوم الفطر، وقيل بل تفريقها في الشهر.

A) Makruh (disliked)

Key Takeaway
The six days of Shawwal are recommended; the later Hanafi consensus allows consecutiveness, settling the intra-madhhab dispute.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Consecutiveness in Obligatory Fasts
Classifies obligatory fasts into those requiring consecutiveness and explains effect of breaking it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify obligatory fasts based on the nature of consecutiveness and determine whether breaking it requires restarting or merely making up the missed day.

Core Idea
Consecutiveness is either a condition of the act or a time requirement; breaking each has different consequences.

Not all obligatory fasts require consecutive days in the same way. In some, the consecutiveness is a condition of the act itself, so breaking it invalidates the entire sequence and demands a fresh start. In others, the consecutiveness is tied to a specific time, so if broken, you simply continue and make up the missed day. This lesson walks you through the Hanafi classification and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of fasts
The text lists thirteen obligatory fasts: seven require consecutiveness (tawabi') and six do not. Note which ones fall into each group.
Examples: Ramadan and expiation fasts require consecutiveness; qada of Ramadan and unspecified vows do not.
2
Distinguish the legal nature of tawabi'
For the seven fasts, tawabi' is either a condition of the act (shart) or a requirement of time. Understand the criterion: if commanded for the sake of the act itself, it is a condition; if commanded because the time is fixed, it is a time requirement.
First type: expiations for killing, zihar, oath, breaking fast; second type: Ramadan, specified vow, specified oath.
3
Apply the rule to breaking scenarios
If tawabi' is a condition (first type), breaking a day means you must restart the entire sequence (istiqbal). If tawabi' is a time requirement (second type), breaking a day means you continue and make up only that day (bina').
The text uses 'استقبال' for restart and 'بناء' for continuation.
4
Recognize exceptions and expansions
The text notes that an unspecified vow (nazr mutlaq) joins the first type if consecutiveness is mentioned or intended. Understand that intention can change the category.
This is an important nuance: the same act can be ruled differently based on intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الصيامات اللازمة فرضاً ثلاثة عشر، بسبعة منها يجب فيها التتابع وهي: رمضان وكفارة القتل وكفارة الظهار وكفارة اليمين وكفارة الإفطار في رمضان والنذر المعين وصوم اليمين المعين وسنة لا يجب فيها التتابع وهي : قضاء رمضان وصوم المتعة وصوم كفارة الحلق وصوم جزاء الصيد وصوم النذر المطلق وصوم اليمين بأن قال والله لأصومن شهراً. ثم أذا أفطر يوماً يجب فيه التتابع هل يلزمه الاستقبال أو لا فنقول : كل صوم يؤمر فيه بالتتابع لأجل الفعل وهو الصوم يكون التتابع شرطاً فيه، وكل صوم يؤمر فيه بالتتابع لأجل أن الوقت مفوت ذلك يسقط التتابع، وإن بقي الفعل واجب القضاء فالأول كصوم كفارة القتل والظهار واليمين والإفطار ويلحق به النذر المطلق إذا ذكر التتابع فيه أو نواه، والثاني كرمضان والنذر المعين واليمين معين . بصوم يوم كذا ذكره صاحب البدائع والأسبيجابي مختصراً، ومحاسنه كثيرة منها شكر النعمة اتي هي المفطرات الثلاثة لأن بضدها تتبين الأشياء، ومنها أنه وسيلة إلى التقوى لأنها إذا انقادت إلى الامتناع عن الحلال طمعاً في مرضاته تعالى فالأولى أن تنقاد للامتناع عن الحرام وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى لعلكم تتقون ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸۳] ومنها كسر الشهوة الداعية إلى المعاصي، ومنها الاتصاف بصفة الملائكة الروحانية، ومنها علمه بحال الفقراء ليرحمهم فيطعمهم، ومنها موافقته لهم . قوله : (يكون التتابع شرطاً فيه) أي فإذا تخلل الفطر في خلاله يلزمه الاستقبال قوله : ( يسقط التتابع) أي فلو أفطر في خلاله لا يستقبل بل يبني على ما فات .
Then know that the obligatory fasts that are binding are thirteen: seven of them require consecutiveness, namely: Ramadan, expiation for killing, expiation for zihar, expiation for a vow, expiation for breaking fast in Ramadan, a specified vow, and a specified oath fast. And six do not require consecutiveness: making up Ramadan, tamattu' fast, expiation for shaving the head, compensation for game, an unspecified vow, and an oath such as saying: 'By Allah, I will fast a month.' Then if one breaks a fast on a day that requires consecutiveness, is it necessary to restart or not? We say: Every fast that is commanded with consecutiveness for the sake of the act itself has consecutiveness as a condition; and every fast that is commanded with consecutiveness because the time is lost, consecutiveness drops, and the act remains obligatory to make up. The first type includes expiation for killing, zihar, oath, and breaking fast, and is joined by an unspecified vow if consecutiveness is mentioned or intended. The second type includes Ramadan, specified vow, and specified oath for a specific day. This is mentioned by the author of al-Bada'i' and al-Asbijabi briefly. And its benefits are many: gratitude for the three types of nullifiers, because opposites clarify things; and it is a means to piety, because if one refrains from the permissible out of desire for Allah's pleasure, it is more fitting to refrain from the forbidden; and to this points His saying: 'that you may become righteous' (2:183); and it breaks desire that leads to sins; and it attains the spiritual qualities of angels; and it informs of the condition of the poor so that one may have mercy on them and feed them; and it agrees with them. [Commentary] His saying: 'consecutiveness is a condition in it' means if breaking fast occurs in the midst, he must restart. His saying: 'consecutiveness drops' means if he breaks fast in the midst, he does not restart but continues with what he missed.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis from the sharh explaining which fasts require consecutiveness (tawabi') and what happens if one breaks the consecutiveness. The author begins by listing thirteen obligatory fasts, dividing them into seven that require consecutiveness and six that do not. The principle is then stated: if consecutiveness is commanded for the sake of the act itself (i.e., the fast itself requires continuity), then breaking the sequence invalidates the past days, and one must start over. But if consecutiveness is commanded because the time is fixed (like Ramadan, which must be in its month, or a specified vow on specific days), then if one breaks the fast, the consecutiveness requirement drops, and the person simply makes up the missed days individually, without needing to restart the entire sequence. Examples are given for each category. The passage then lists several benefits of fasting, including gratitude, piety, controlling desire, and empathy for the poor. The commentary at the end clarifies the two sub-rules: if consecutiveness is a condition, breaking requires restart; if it is merely a requirement of the time, then one can continue (build) on what was already done.

Key Terms
التتابع (al-tawabi'): Consecutive days of fasting without a break.
الاستقبال (al-istiqbal): Starting over from the beginning of the fast sequence.
البناء (al-bina'): Continuing from where one left off after a break, without restarting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone breaks the consecutiveness of a fast that requires tawabi' as a condition of the act itself, such as the expiation for zihar, what must he do?
A) Continue from where he left off
B) Start over from the beginning
C) Make up only the missed day
D) No need to do anything

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, consecutiveness in fasting is either a condition requiring restart if broken, or a time requirement allowing continuation with make-up of missed days.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner

The Technical Definition of Fasting: Imsak as Active Abstention
Defines fasting technically and explains the preference for 'imsak'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasting, explain why 'imsak' is preferred over 'tark', and identify the conditions required for a valid fast.

Core Idea
Fasting is active refraining (imsak) from nullifiers, not mere passivity; intention distinguishes worship from habit.

This lesson unpacks the precise legal definition of fasting according to the Hanafi school. The text from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that the definition given by al-Quduri uses 'imsak' instead of 'tark' to emphasize that the fast is an act of the responsible person. We will examine what this means in practice and how it affects who is obligated and what constitutes a valid fast.

Guided Path
1
State the definition
Identify the three elements of the definition: nullifiers (eating, drinking, sexual intercourse), time (from dawn to sunset), and person (the responsible person with intention).
The definition is the starting point for understanding the act of fasting.
2
Understand 'imsak' vs 'tark'
Explain why the commentator prefers 'imsak' (active refraining) over 'tark' (abstention) – because obligation requires a possible act, and 'tark' is the absence of action which is not under one's control.
This is a key Hanafi usul principle: obligation only applies to what is within human capacity.
3
Include constructive abstinence
Explain the 'hukmi' aspect that includes the forgetful eater as still fasting; they are considered 'refraining constructively' (imsak hukman).
This shows legal flexibility based on the reality of the act.
4
Identify conditions of validity
List the conditions mentioned: being Muslim, free from menstruation/postpartum bleeding, sanity, and intention. The term 'its people' (ahluhu) excludes those who do not meet these conditions.
Only those who are eligible can perform a valid fast.
5
Clarify what breaks the fast
Anything that enters the stomach, even non-food, breaks the fast if done intentionally. However, water reaching the brain is considered entering the stomach due to the passage between them, so it also breaks the fast.
This refines the definition of 'eating' to include any substance entering the stomach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هو ترك الأكل والشرب والجماع من الصبح إلى الغروب بنية من أهله) أي الصوم في الشرع الإمساك عن المفطرات الثلاث حقيقة أو حكماً في وقت مخصوص من شخص مخصوص مع النية، وإنما فسرنا الترك بالإمساك المذكور في كلام القدوري ليكون فعل المكلف لأنه لا تكليف إلا بفعل حتى قالوا إن المكلف به في النهي كف النفس لا ترك الفعل لأنه لا تكليف إلا بمقدور والمعدوم غير مقدور لأن تفسير القادر بمن إن شاء فعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل لا وإن شاء ،ترك، وتمامه في تحرير الأصول وقلنا حقيقة وحكماً ليدخل من أفطر ناسياً فإنه ممسك حكماً، واختص الصوم باليوم لتعذر الوصال المنهي عنه وكونه على خلاف العادة وعليه مبنى العبادة إذ ترك الأكل بالليل ،معتاد واشترطت النية لتمييز العبادة العادة كما سيأتي. وأراد بالأهل من اجتمعت فيه شروط الصحة وتقدم أنها ثلاثة فخرج الكافر والحائض والنفساء والمراد باشتراط الطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس اشتراط عدمهما لا أن يكون المراد منها الاغتسال كذا في النهاية . والمراد بترك الأكل ترك إدخال شيء بطنه أعم من كونه مأكولاً أولا لما سيأتي من إبطاله بإدخال نحو الحديد، ولا يرد ما وصل إلى الدماغ فإنه مفطر كما سيأتي لما أن بين الدماغ والجوف منفذاً فما وصل إلى الدماغ وصل إلى الجوف كما صرح به في البدائع على ما سيأتي وفي البزازية : استنشق فوصل الماء إلى فمه ولم يصل إلى دماغه لا يفسد صومه. قوله : (وصح صوم رمضان والنذر المعين والنفل بنية من الليل إلى ما قبل نصف النهار) شروع في بيان النية التي شرط الصحة لكل صوم وعرفها في المحيط بأن يعرف بقلبه ووقتها بعد الغروب ولا يجوز قبله والتسحر نية كذا في الظهيرية. ولم يتكلم على فرضية رمضان لما أنها من الاعتقادات لا لفقه لثبوتها بالقطعي المتأيد بالإجماع ولهذا يحكم بكفر جاحده. وكانت فرضيته بعد ما صرفت القبلة إلى الكعبة بشهر في شعبان على رأس ثمانية عشر شهراً من الهجرة وهو في الأصل من رمض إذا احترق به لأن الذنوب تحترق فيه، وهو غير منصرف للعلمية والألف والنون قال الجوهري : يجمع على ارمضا ورمضانات. وقال الفراء : يجمع على رماضين كسلاطين وشياطين. وقال ابن الأنباري : رماض جمع رمضان. وتقدم حكم النذر أنه فرض على الأظهر، والمراد بالنفل ما عدا
His saying: (It is abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention from its people) i.e., fasting in the Law is refraining from the three nullifiers, literally or constructively, at a specific time, by a specific person, with intention. We interpreted 'abstention' as 'refraining' mentioned in the speech of al-Quduri to make it an act of the responsible person, because there is no obligation except for an act, so they said: what is commanded in prohibitions is restraining oneself, not the absence of the act, because there is no obligation except for something possible, and non-existence is not possible, because the definition of the capable is: if he wills he does, if he does not will he does not do, not 'if he wills he leaves'. Its full discussion is in Tahrir al-Usul. And we said 'literally or constructively' to include one who breaks fasting forgetfully, for he is refraining constructively. And fasting is specific to the daytime because continuous fasting (wisal) is prohibited and because night abstinence is customary; worship is based on that. Intention is required to distinguish worship from habit, as will come. And by 'its people' he means those who fulfill the conditions of validity, which were mentioned as three, so the disbeliever, menstruating woman, and woman in postpartum bleeding are excluded. And the requirement of purity from menstruation and postpartum means the absence of them, not that bathing is meant, as in al-Nihaya. And by 'abstention from eating' means not introducing anything into the stomach, whether it is food or not, because of what will come about invalidating by inserting something like iron. And there is no objection to what reaches the brain, for it breaks the fast as will come, because between the brain and the stomach there is a passage, so what reaches the brain reaches the stomach, as stated in al-Bada'i'. In al-Bazzaziyya: If one sniffs and water reaches his mouth but not his brain, his fast is not invalidated. His saying: (The fast of Ramadan, a specified vow, and supererogatory fasts are valid with intention from the night until before noon) is a start to explain the intention that is a condition of validity for every fast. Al-Muhit defines it as knowing in one's heart; its time is after sunset and it is not valid before. And pre-dawn meal (suhur) counts as intention, as in al-Zahiriyya. He did not discuss the obligation of Ramadan because it is a matter of belief, not fiqh, due to its being established by definitive evidence supported by consensus, so one who denies it is judged an unbeliever. Its obligation was after the qibla was turned to the Ka'aba, one month later in Sha'ban, eighteen months after the Hijra. It is derived from 'ramad' meaning burning, because sins are burnt in it. It is indeclinable due to being proper noun and having alif and nun. Al-Jawhari said: It is pluralized as 'armida' and 'ramadanaat'. Al-Farra' said: It is pluralized as 'ramadin' like 'salatin' and 'shayateen'. Ibn al-Anbari said: 'ramaad' is plural of 'Ramadan'. The ruling of a vow has preceded, that it is obligatory on the strongest view. And by 'supererogatory (nafl)' is meant other than [the obligatory].
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of fasting from the sharh, explaining the terminology. The author begins by explaining the text's definition: fasting is abstaining from the three nullifiers (eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse) from dawn to sunset with intention from the eligible person. The commentary then refines this definition: instead of saying 'abstention' (tark), the author prefers 'refraining' (imsak) because it is an act within human power, whereas 'not doing' is not an act. This is a key legal principle: obligations are only for possible actions. The definition includes constructive abstinence to cover the forgetful person, who is legally considered as fasting. Intention is required to distinguish worship from habit. The term 'its people' (ahluhu) refers to those who meet the conditions of validity for fasting: being Muslim, not menstruating, and being sane/mature. The passage then discusses the meaning of 'eating': it includes anything that enters the stomach, even non-food items. However, if water reaches the brain via sniffing, it breaks the fast because the brain is connected to the stomach. The second part deals with the timing of intention. For Ramadan, specified vows, and supererogatory fasts, intention can be made from the night (after sunset) up to before noon (i.e., before half the day passes). The commentary explains that this is because the obligation of Ramadan is definitive (qat'i) and thus does not require fiqh discussion. It also gives the etymology of 'Ramadan' and its plural forms. The passage concludes that the ruling on vows is that it is obligatory on the strongest view.

Key Terms
الإمساك (al-imsak): The active act of refraining from nullifiers; preferred over 'tark' because it is an act within human power.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention; defined as knowing in one's heart. Required for validity of fasting. Time: after sunset and before dawn for most fasts; for Ramadan, specified vow, and nafl, it can be extended until before noon.
الحكمي (al-hukmi): Constructive or legal reality; e.g., a person who breaks the fast forgetfully is considered refraining 'constructively' (hukman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal effect if someone eats forgetfully during Ramadan according to the Hanafi school?
A) Fast is invalid and must be repeated.
B) Fast is valid and no compensation.
C) Fast is valid but must be compensated.
D) Fast is makruh.

Key Takeaway
Fasting in the Hanafi school is an active act of refraining (imsak) with intention, not mere absence of action.

The Timing of Intention (Niyyah) for Fasting
Explains the timing of niyyah for different fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the timing requirements for niyyah for different types of fasts and explain the rationale for extending the time for Ramadan, specified vows, and nafl.

Core Idea
For Ramadan and nafl, intention is valid until noon; for other obligatory fasts, it must be made before dawn.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage, which specifies the permissible time for making the intention for fasting. Intention is a condition for validity, but its timing varies depending on the type of fast. Understanding this flexibility is crucial for proper practice.

Guided Path
1
Recognize that intention is required for every fast
Intention (niyyah) is a condition (shart) for the validity of all fasting. It is defined as knowing in one's heart.
Without intention, the act is not considered worship.
2
Identify the three types of fasts mentioned
The three types are: Ramadan fast, a specified vowed fast (nadhr mu'ayyan, e.g., 'I will fast this specific day for Allah'), and supererogatory (nafl) fast.
These three share a relaxed intention time.
3
State the time window for these three
Intention can be made from the night (after sunset) until before noon (i.e., before half the day passes). This includes before dawn (suhur) and after dawn up to noon.
Suhur counts as intention, as stated in al-Zahiriyya.
4
Note the requirement for other obligatory fasts
For other obligatory fasts (like qada of Ramadan, expiation, or unspecified vows), intention must be made at night before dawn. This is implied because only the three are mentioned as having an extended time.
The passage does not explicitly state this, but it is the understanding of the madhhab.
5
Understand the rationale for not discussing Ramadan's obligation
The passage notes that the obligation of Ramadan is not discussed in fiqh because it is established by definitive evidence (Quran, ijma), and denial constitutes disbelief.
This highlights the role of usul al-fiqh in determining levels of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هو ترك الأكل والشرب والجماع من الصبح إلى الغروب بنية من أهله) أي الصوم في الشرع الإمساك عن المفطرات الثلاث حقيقة أو حكماً في وقت مخصوص من شخص مخصوص مع النية، وإنما فسرنا الترك بالإمساك المذكور في كلام القدوري ليكون فعل المكلف لأنه لا تكليف إلا بفعل حتى قالوا إن المكلف به في النهي كف النفس لا ترك الفعل لأنه لا تكليف إلا بمقدور والمعدوم غير مقدور لأن تفسير القادر بمن إن شاء فعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل لا وإن شاء ،ترك، وتمامه في تحرير الأصول وقلنا حقيقة وحكماً ليدخل من أفطر ناسياً فإنه ممسك حكماً، واختص الصوم باليوم لتعذر الوصال المنهي عنه وكونه على خلاف العادة وعليه مبنى العبادة إذ ترك الأكل بالليل ،معتاد واشترطت النية لتمييز العبادة العادة كما سيأتي. وأراد بالأهل من اجتمعت فيه شروط الصحة وتقدم أنها ثلاثة فخرج الكافر والحائض والنفساء والمراد باشتراط الطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس اشتراط عدمهما لا أن يكون المراد منها الاغتسال كذا في النهاية . والمراد بترك الأكل ترك إدخال شيء بطنه أعم من كونه مأكولاً أولا لما سيأتي من إبطاله بإدخال نحو الحديد، ولا يرد ما وصل إلى الدماغ فإنه مفطر كما سيأتي لما أن بين الدماغ والجوف منفذاً فما وصل إلى الدماغ وصل إلى الجوف كما صرح به في البدائع على ما سيأتي وفي البزازية : استنشق فوصل الماء إلى فمه ولم يصل إلى دماغه لا يفسد صومه. قوله : (وصح صوم رمضان والنذر المعين والنفل بنية من الليل إلى ما قبل نصف النهار) شروع في بيان النية التي شرط الصحة لكل صوم وعرفها في المحيط بأن يعرف بقلبه ووقتها بعد الغروب ولا يجوز قبله والتسحر نية كذا في الظهيرية. ولم يتكلم على فرضية رمضان لما أنها من الاعتقادات لا لفقه لثبوتها بالقطعي المتأيد بالإجماع ولهذا يحكم بكفر جاحده. وكانت فرضيته بعد ما صرفت القبلة إلى الكعبة بشهر في شعبان على رأس ثمانية عشر شهراً من الهجرة وهو في الأصل من رمض إذا احترق به لأن الذنوب تحترق فيه، وهو غير منصرف للعلمية والألف والنون قال الجوهري : يجمع على ارمضا ورمضانات. وقال الفراء : يجمع على رماضين كسلاطين وشياطين. وقال ابن الأنباري : رماض جمع رمضان. وتقدم حكم النذر أنه فرض على الأظهر، والمراد بالنفل ما عدا
His saying: (It is abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention from its people) i.e., fasting in the Law is refraining from the three nullifiers, literally or constructively, at a specific time, by a specific person, with intention. We interpreted 'abstention' as 'refraining' mentioned in the speech of al-Quduri to make it an act of the responsible person, because there is no obligation except for an act, so they said: what is commanded in prohibitions is restraining oneself, not the absence of the act, because there is no obligation except for something possible, and non-existence is not possible, because the definition of the capable is: if he wills he does, if he does not will he does not do, not 'if he wills he leaves'. Its full discussion is in Tahrir al-Usul. And we said 'literally or constructively' to include one who breaks fasting forgetfully, for he is refraining constructively. And fasting is specific to the daytime because continuous fasting (wisal) is prohibited and because night abstinence is customary; worship is based on that. Intention is required to distinguish worship from habit, as will come. And by 'its people' he means those who fulfill the conditions of validity, which were mentioned as three, so the disbeliever, menstruating woman, and woman in postpartum bleeding are excluded. And the requirement of purity from menstruation and postpartum means the absence of them, not that bathing is meant, as in al-Nihaya. And by 'abstention from eating' means not introducing anything into the stomach, whether it is food or not, because of what will come about invalidating by inserting something like iron. And there is no objection to what reaches the brain, for it breaks the fast as will come, because between the brain and the stomach there is a passage, so what reaches the brain reaches the stomach, as stated in al-Bada'i'. In al-Bazzaziyya: If one sniffs and water reaches his mouth but not his brain, his fast is not invalidated. His saying: (The fast of Ramadan, a specified vow, and supererogatory fasts are valid with intention from the night until before noon) is a start to explain the intention that is a condition of validity for every fast. Al-Muhit defines it as knowing in one's heart; its time is after sunset and it is not valid before. And pre-dawn meal (suhur) counts as intention, as in al-Zahiriyya. He did not discuss the obligation of Ramadan because it is a matter of belief, not fiqh, due to its being established by definitive evidence supported by consensus, so one who denies it is judged an unbeliever. Its obligation was after the qibla was turned to the Ka'aba, one month later in Sha'ban, eighteen months after the Hijra. It is derived from 'ramad' meaning burning, because sins are burnt in it. It is indeclinable due to being proper noun and having alif and nun. Al-Jawhari said: It is pluralized as 'armida' and 'ramadanaat'. Al-Farra' said: It is pluralized as 'ramadin' like 'salatin' and 'shayateen'. Ibn al-Anbari said: 'ramaad' is plural of 'Ramadan'. The ruling of a vow has preceded, that it is obligatory on the strongest view. And by 'supererogatory (nafl)' is meant other than [the obligatory].
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of fasting from the sharh, explaining the terminology. The author begins by explaining the text's definition: fasting is abstaining from the three nullifiers (eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse) from dawn to sunset with intention from the eligible person. The commentary then refines this definition: instead of saying 'abstention' (tark), the author prefers 'refraining' (imsak) because it is an act within human power, whereas 'not doing' is not an act. This is a key legal principle: obligations are only for possible actions. The definition includes constructive abstinence to cover the forgetful person, who is legally considered as fasting. Intention is required to distinguish worship from habit. The term 'its people' (ahluhu) refers to those who meet the conditions of validity for fasting: being Muslim, not menstruating, and being sane/mature. The passage then discusses the meaning of 'eating': it includes anything that enters the stomach, even non-food items. However, if water reaches the brain via sniffing, it breaks the fast because the brain is connected to the stomach. The second part deals with the timing of intention. For Ramadan, specified vows, and supererogatory fasts, intention can be made from the night (after sunset) up to before noon (i.e., before half the day passes). The commentary explains that this is because the obligation of Ramadan is definitive (qat'i) and thus does not require fiqh discussion. It also gives the etymology of 'Ramadan' and its plural forms. The passage concludes that the ruling on vows is that it is obligatory on the strongest view.

Key Terms
الإمساك (al-imsak): The active act of refraining from nullifiers; preferred over 'tark' because it is an act within human power.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention; defined as knowing in one's heart. Required for validity of fasting. Time: after sunset and before dawn for most fasts; for Ramadan, specified vow, and nafl, it can be extended until before noon.
الحكمي (al-hukmi): Constructive or legal reality; e.g., a person who breaks the fast forgetfully is considered refraining 'constructively' (hukman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For which fasts can intention be made after dawn until before noon?
A) Only Ramadan
B) Ramadan, specified vow, and nafl
C) All obligatory fasts
D) Only nafl

Key Takeaway
The timing of intention for fasting varies by type, with greater flexibility for Ramadan, specified vows, and voluntary fasts.

Definition of Fasting in the Hanafi School
Defining fasting and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasting and identify the conditions required for its validity.

Core Idea
Fasting is an intentional act of abstaining from nullifiers, not mere non-doing.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the definition of fasting given in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. We will explore how fasting is defined as an act of the mukallaf, the conditions for validity, and why intention is essential to separate worship from habit.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition components
Note the four elements: abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse; from dawn to sunset; with intention.
2
Analyze 'abstention' as an act
The text explains that abstention is a conscious act of restraining oneself, not merely the absence of action. This is important because obligations must be performable acts.
See the discussion of 'imsak' vs 'tark'.
3
Identify the conditions of validity
The text mentions three conditions: being Muslim, free from menstruation/postpartum bleeding, and having intention.
These exclude disbelievers, menstruating women, etc.
4
Understand the role of intention
Intention distinguishes worship from mere habit. The timing of intention is discussed in the next lesson.

And it is valid: it is the abstinence from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention from its people. The fast of Ramadan, specified vow, and supererogatory [fast] are valid with intention from the night until before half of the day, and with absolute intention. About his saying: (It is the abstinence from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention from its people) meaning fasting in Sacred Law is the refraining from the three nullifiers, literally or legally, in a specific time from a specific person with intention. We interpreted 'abstinence' as the refraining mentioned in al-Quduri's words to make it an act of the responsible person because obligation is only by act. They said that what is required in a prohibition is restraining the self, not leaving the act, because obligation is only upon what is possible, and non-existence is not possible. The explanation of the capable is one who if he wills does, and if he does not will does not do, not 'if he wills leaves'. The complete discussion is in Tahrir al-Usul. We said 'literally or legally' to include one who forgets and eats, for he is legally refraining. The fast is specific to the day due to the difficulty of continuous fasting which is prohibited, and because it is contrary to habit, and worship is based on that, for leaving food at night is habitual. Intention is required to distinguish worship from habit as will come. By 'its people' he means one in whom the conditions of validity are found, and we previously stated they are three. So the disbeliever, menstruating woman, and woman in postpartum bleeding are excluded. The requirement of purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding means the absence of them, not that ritual washing is intended, as in al-Nihayah. By 'abstinence from eating' is meant refraining from entering anything into the stomach, whether it is food or not, because of what will come that fasting is invalidated by inserting something like iron. It is not objected that what reaches the brain is a nullifier as will come, because there is a passage between the brain and the stomach, so what reaches the brain reaches the stomach as stated in al-Bada'i'. In al-Bazzaziyya: if one sniffs and water reaches his mouth but does not reach his brain, his fast is not invalidated. His saying: (And the fast of Ramadan, specified vow, and supererogatory are valid with intention from the night until before half of the day) is a beginning in explaining the intention which is a condition of validity for every fast. Al-Muhit defines it as knowing with the heart, its time is after sunset and it is not permissible before, and taking suhur is considered intention as in al-Zahiriyya. He did not discuss the obligation of Ramadan because it is among beliefs, not jurisprudence, due to its establishment by definitive proof supported by consensus, so its denier is deemed a disbeliever. Its obligation came after the qibla was turned to the Kaaba in Sha'ban, eighteen months after the Hijra. The word Ramadan is derived from ramad meaning burning, because sins are burned in it. It is indeclinable due to being proper and having alif and nun. Al-Jawhari said: it is pluralized as armida and ramadanat. Al-Farra said: it is pluralized as ramadin like salatin and shayatin. Ibn al-Anbari said: ramad is the plural of ramadan. The ruling of a vow is that it is obligatory on the most apparent view. By supererogatory is meant what is other than obligatory, and the obligatory is more general, including sunna, recommended, or disliked. He indicated that if one intends at sunset, his intention is not valid because it is before the time, as we have mentioned. In Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: if one intends at the end of the night and then wakes up fasting, this intention is not valid, just as if he intended after asr for the next day's fast. Al-Tahawi used as evidence for not requiring night intention in Ramadan the hadith in the two Sahihs: 'on a day, whoever had eaten should complete the rest of his day, and whoever had not eaten should fast.' And his fast was obligatory until Ramadan was prescribed, then it became sunna. So it contains evidence that one who is obligated to fast a day and did not intend it at night, his intention during the day suffices. Therefore, the hadith of the four Sunan 'no fast for one who does not intend fasting from the night' must be interpreted as negating perfection, because the best in every fast is to intend at the time of dawn if possible or from the night as in al-Bada'i', or that the meaning is 'he did not intend that the fast be from the night' so the preposition 'from the night' is attached to the second 'fast' not 'intend'. The conclusion is: no fast for one who did not intend that he is fasting from the night i.e. from its last parts, so it is a negation of the validity of the fast from the time he intended during the day. And on the assumption that it is for negating validity, its generality must be specified by what we have narrated with them. With us, if it were definitive, it would have been specified by some, so it would be specified by some. How can it be that it has both non-probability and specification when supererogatory has been specified from it by the hadith of Muslim from Aisha: 'The Messenger of Allah entered upon me and said: Do you have anything? We said: No. He said: Then I am fasting.' The day that Ashura fasting is original, and Ramadan fast and specified vow fast are attached to it in the ruling, which is not requiring intention from the night. Its consequence is attaching every obligatory fast to it, but qiyas only serves to specify a report, not to abrogate it. If we were to follow the full implication of this qiyas, it would abrogate the hadith of the Sunan since nothing would remain under it. So it must be limited to the context of the text, which is the specific obligatory days of Ramadan and its equivalent from specified vows. It is not possible to cancel the condition of specification in the context of the text we narrated, for that would be nullifying the ruling of a word without a word with a text. Rather, the consideration of intention being present in most of the day is specific because what we narrated from the hadith of the two Sahihs is a specific incident and not general to all parts of the day. It is possible that the permission to fast in that incident was due to the presence of intention in most of it, or it is possible that it was for absolute permission during the day. As will come regarding invalidating by inserting something like iron. If the author had said, as in al-Fath, 'It is refraining from intercourse and from entering anything into the stomach or what has the ruling of the internal, from dawn to sunset, with intention', it would have been better.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion (sharh) of the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author first defines fasting (sawm) in Sacred Law as intentional abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from true dawn until sunset. He clarifies that 'abstention' is an act of the responsible person (mukallaf) because obligations must be performable acts; hence, it means restraining oneself from nullifiers, not merely 'not doing' something. The intention (niyyah) is required to distinguish worship from habit. Fasting is specific to daytime because night is the normal time for eating. The commentator then explains conditions for the validity of fasting: being Muslim, free from menstruation/postpartum bleeding, etc. He then focuses on the timing of intention. For Ramadan, specified vow, and supererogatory fasts, intention may be made from after sunset (or after fajr according to some) until before half of the day (i.e., before zawal). This is a distinctive Hanafi ruling. The main dispute is with the Shafi'i school, which requires night intention (tabyit) for all obligatory fasts. The Hanafi proof is the hadith in Bukhari and Muslim: the Prophet commanded a man who had eaten in Ramadan to complete the rest of the day, and another who had not eaten to fast. This shows that intention after fajr is valid for a compulsory fast. The second hadith 'No fast for the one who does not intend it from the night' is interpreted by Hanafis as indicating perfection (kamal) rather than validity, or as specific to non-Ramadan obligatory fasts. The commentator refutes the Shafi'i interpretation by arguing that qiyas can specify a general hadith but cannot abrogate it, so the Ramadan fast is an exception. The Hanafi position is that intention is valid until before half the day, and for supererogatory fasts even later (with absolute intention). The passage ends by suggesting a more precise definition of fasting.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): In Sacred Law: intentional abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from true dawn to sunset with intention.
النية (al-niyyah): The mental determination to perform an act of worship, distinguishing it from habit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the Hanafi definition of fasting include besides abstention from nullifiers?
A) Intention
B) Time from dawn to dusk
C) Both intention and specified time
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Fasting is an intentional act of abstaining from nullifiers from dawn to sunset, requiring intention as a condition.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate

Timing of Intention for Fasting – The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Debate on necessity of night intention for Ramadan fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the timing of intention for Ramadan and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument using the dalil chain.

Core Idea
Intentions for Ramadan can be made during the day before half the day, contrary to the Shafi'i requirement of night intention.

The text now addresses a central difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: when must the intention for the Ramadan fast be made? The commentator resolves the conflict of hadiths to establish the Hanafi view that daytime intention suffices. This lesson will examine both proofs and the Hanafi reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule
Intention for Ramadan, nadhr mu'ayyan, and nafl may be made from night until before half the day (zawal).
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
They require tabyit (night intention) for all obligatory fasts, including Ramadan.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The hadith in the Sahihayn: the Prophet commanded a man who had eaten in Ramadan to complete the rest of the day, showing daytime intention is valid.
This is a specific incident (shakhs) in Ramadan.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
The hadith: 'No fast for the one who does not intend from the night' (la siyama liman lam yanawi min al-layl).
This is general (amm).
5
Understand the Hanafi response
The Sunan hadith is interpreted as recommending perfection (kamal) or is specified (takhsis) by the Sahihayn hadith. Qiyas on nafl and Ashura supports the Hanafi view.
The principle: takhsis over naskh.

الغروب بنية من أهله) أي الصوم في الشرع الإمساك عن المفطرات الثلاث حقيقة أو حكماً في وقت مخصوص من شخص مخصوص مع النية، وإنما فسرنا الترك بالإمساك المذكور في كلام القدوري ليكون فعل المكلف لأنه لا تكليف وقلنا حقيقة وحكماً ليدخل من أفطر ناسياً فإنه ممسك حكماً، واختص الصوم باليوم لتعذر الوصال المنهي عنه وكونه على خلاف العادة وعليه مبنى العبادة إذ ترك الأكل بالليل معتاد واشترطت النية لتمييز العبادة عن العادة كما سيأتي. وأراد بالأهل من اجتمعت فيه شروط الصحة وتقدم أنها ثلاثة فخرج الكافر والحائض والنفساء والمراد باشتراط الطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس اشتراط عدمهما لا أن يكون المراد منها الاغتسال كذا في النهاية . والمراد بترك الأكل ترك إدخال شيء بطنه أعم من كونه مأكولاً أولا لما سيأتي من إبطاله بإدخال نحو الحديد، ولا يرد ما وصل إلى الدماغ فإنه مفطر كما سيأتي لما أن بين الدماغ والجوف منفذاً فما وصل إلى الدماغ وصل إلى الجوف كما صرح به في البدائع على ما سيأتي وفي البزازية : استنشق فوصل الماء إلى فمه ولم يصل إلى دماغه لا يفسد صومه. قوله : (وصح صوم رمضان والنذر المعين والنفل بنية من الليل إلى ما قبل نصف النهار) شروع في بيان النية التي شرط الصحة لكل صوم وعرفها في المحيط بأن يعرف بقلبه ووقتها بعد الغروب ولا يجوز قبله والتسحر نية كذا في الظهيرية. ولم يتكلم على فرضية رمضان لما أنها من الاعتقادات لا لفقه لثبوتها بالقطعي المتأيد بالإجماع ولهذا يحكم بكفر جاحده. وكانت فرضيته بعد ما صرفت القبلة إلى الكعبة بشهر في شعبان على رأس ثمانية عشر شهراً من الهجرة وهو في الأصل من رمض إذا احترق به لأن الذنوب تحترق فيه، وهو غير منصرف للعلمية والألف والنون قال الجوهري : يجمع على ارمضا ورمضانات. وقال الفراء : يجمع على رماضين كسلاطين وشياطين. وقال ابن الأنباري : رماض جمع رمضان. وتقدم حكم النذر أنه فرض على الأظهر، والمراد بالنفل ما عدا الفرض، والواجب أعم من أن يكون سنة أو مندوباً أو مكروهاً. وأشار إلى أنه لو نوى عند الغروب لا تصح نيته لأنه قبل الوقت كما قدمناه وفي فتاوى الظهيرية : ولو نوى أن في آخر الليل ثم يصبح صائماً لم تصح هذه النية كما لو نوى بعد العصر صوم الغد ا هـ . واستدل الطحاوي لعدم اشتراط التبييت في رمضان بحديث الصحيحين في يوم من أكل فليمسك بقية يومه ومن لم يكن أكل فليصم (۱) وكان صومه فرضاً حتى فرض رمضان فصار سنة ففيه دليل على أن من تعين عليه صوم يوم ولم ينوه ليلاً تجزئه النية نهاراً فوجب حمل حديث السنن الأربعة لا صيام لمن لم ينو الصيام من الليل (۲) على نفي الكمال لأن الأفضل في كل صوم أن ينوي وقت طلوع الفجر إن أمكنه أو من الليل كما في البدائع، أو على أن المراد لم ينو كون الصوم من الليل فيكون الجار وهو من الليل متعلقاً بصيام الثاني لا ينوي، فحاصله لا صيام لمن لم يقصد أنه صائم من الليل أي من آخر أجزائه فيكون نفياً لصحة الصوم من حين نوى من النهار وعلى تقدير كونه لنفي الصحة وجب أن يخص عمومه بما روينا عندهم وعندنا لو كان قطعياً خص بعضه خصص به بعض فكيف وقد اجتمع فيه عدم الظنية والتخصيص إذ قد خصص منه النفل بحديث مسلم عن عائشة : دخل علي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ذات فقال: هل عندكم شيء؟ فقلنا : لا . فقال : إني إذاً صائم. يوم أن صوم عاشوراء أصل وألحق به صوم رمضان والمنذور المعين في حكمه وهو عدم النية من الليل ومقتضاه إلحاق كل صوم واجب به لكن القياس إنما يصلح مخصصاً للخبر لا ناسخاً، ولو جرينا على تمام لازم هذه القياس لكان ناسخاً لحديث السنن إذ لم يبق تحته شيء حينئذ فوجب أن يحاذى به مورد النص وهو الواجب المعين من رمضان، ونظيره من النذر المعين. ولا يمكن أن يلغي قيد التعيين في مورد النص الذي رويناه فإنه حينئذ يكون إبطالاً لحكم لفظ بلا لفظ بنص فيه . وإنما اختص اعتبارها بوجودها في أكثر النهار لأن ما رويناه من حديث الصحيحين واقعة حال لا عموم لها في جميع أجزاء النهار، واحتمل كون إجازة الصوم في تلك الواقعة لوجود النية فيها في أكثره، واحتمل كونها للتجويز في النهار مطلقاً، كما سيأتي بإفطاره بإدخال نحو الحديد، فلو قال المصنف كما في الفتح هو إمساك عن الجماع وعن إدخال شيء بطناً أو ماله حكم الباطن من الفجر إلى الغروب عن نية لكان أجود.

(۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الصوم باب ۲۱ ٦۹ مسلم في كتاب الصيام حديث ١٣٣. النسائي في كتاب الصيام باب .٦٦ الدارمي في كتاب الصيام باب ٤٦.
(۲) رواه ابن ماجه في كتاب الصيام باب .٢٦ بلفظ ولا صيام لمن لم يفرضه من الليل، ورواه أبو داود في كتاب الصيام باب ۷۱ النسائي في كتاب الصيام باب ٦٨ . أحمد في مسندم (٦ / ٢٨٧) بلفظ من لم يجمع الصيام قبل الفجر فلا صيام له».
And it is valid: it is the abstinence from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention from its people. The fast of Ramadan, specified vow, and supererogatory [fast] are valid with intention from the night until before half of the day, and with absolute intention. About his saying: (It is the abstinence from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention from its people) meaning fasting in Sacred Law is the refraining from the three nullifiers, literally or legally, in a specific time from a specific person with intention. We interpreted 'abstinence' as the refraining mentioned in al-Quduri's words to make it an act of the responsible person because obligation is only by act. They said that what is required in a prohibition is restraining the self, not leaving the act, because obligation is only upon what is possible, and non-existence is not possible. The explanation of the capable is one who if he wills does, and if he does not will does not do, not 'if he wills leaves'. The complete discussion is in Tahrir al-Usul. We said 'literally or legally' to include one who forgets and eats, for he is legally refraining. The fast is specific to the day due to the difficulty of continuous fasting which is prohibited, and because it is contrary to habit, and worship is based on that, for leaving food at night is habitual. Intention is required to distinguish worship from habit as will come. By 'its people' he means one in whom the conditions of validity are found, and we previously stated they are three. So the disbeliever, menstruating woman, and woman in postpartum bleeding are excluded. The requirement of purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding means the absence of them, not that ritual washing is intended, as in al-Nihayah. By 'abstinence from eating' is meant refraining from entering anything into the stomach, whether it is food or not, because of what will come that fasting is invalidated by inserting something like iron. It is not objected that what reaches the brain is a nullifier as will come, because there is a passage between the brain and the stomach, so what reaches the brain reaches the stomach as stated in al-Bada'i'. In al-Bazzaziyya: if one sniffs and water reaches his mouth but does not reach his brain, his fast is not invalidated. His saying: (And the fast of Ramadan, specified vow, and supererogatory are valid with intention from the night until before half of the day) is a beginning in explaining the intention which is a condition of validity for every fast. Al-Muhit defines it as knowing with the heart, its time is after sunset and it is not permissible before, and taking suhur is considered intention as in al-Zahiriyya. He did not discuss the obligation of Ramadan because it is among beliefs, not jurisprudence, due to its establishment by definitive proof supported by consensus, so its denier is deemed a disbeliever. Its obligation came after the qibla was turned to the Kaaba in Sha'ban, eighteen months after the Hijra. The word Ramadan is derived from ramad meaning burning, because sins are burned in it. It is indeclinable due to being proper and having alif and nun. Al-Jawhari said: it is pluralized as armida and ramadanat. Al-Farra said: it is pluralized as ramadin like salatin and shayatin. Ibn al-Anbari said: ramad is the plural of ramadan. The ruling of a vow is that it is obligatory on the most apparent view. By supererogatory is meant what is other than obligatory, and the obligatory is more general, including sunna, recommended, or disliked. He indicated that if one intends at sunset, his intention is not valid because it is before the time, as we have mentioned. In Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: if one intends at the end of the night and then wakes up fasting, this intention is not valid, just as if he intended after asr for the next day's fast. Al-Tahawi used as evidence for not requiring night intention in Ramadan the hadith in the two Sahihs: 'on a day, whoever had eaten should complete the rest of his day, and whoever had not eaten should fast.' And his fast was obligatory until Ramadan was prescribed, then it became sunna. So it contains evidence that one who is obligated to fast a day and did not intend it at night, his intention during the day suffices. Therefore, the hadith of the four Sunan 'no fast for one who does not intend fasting from the night' must be interpreted as negating perfection, because the best in every fast is to intend at the time of dawn if possible or from the night as in al-Bada'i', or that the meaning is 'he did not intend that the fast be from the night' so the preposition 'from the night' is attached to the second 'fast' not 'intend'. The conclusion is: no fast for one who did not intend that he is fasting from the night i.e. from its last parts, so it is a negation of the validity of the fast from the time he intended during the day. And on the assumption that it is for negating validity, its generality must be specified by what we have narrated with them. With us, if it were definitive, it would have been specified by some, so it would be specified by some. How can it be that it has both non-probability and specification when supererogatory has been specified from it by the hadith of Muslim from Aisha: 'The Messenger of Allah entered upon me and said: Do you have anything? We said: No. He said: Then I am fasting.' The day that Ashura fasting is original, and Ramadan fast and specified vow fast are attached to it in the ruling, which is not requiring intention from the night. Its consequence is attaching every obligatory fast to it, but qiyas only serves to specify a report, not to abrogate it. If we were to follow the full implication of this qiyas, it would abrogate the hadith of the Sunan since nothing would remain under it. So it must be limited to the context of the text, which is the specific obligatory days of Ramadan and its equivalent from specified vows. It is not possible to cancel the condition of specification in the context of the text we narrated, for that would be nullifying the ruling of a word without a word with a text. Rather, the consideration of intention being present in most of the day is specific because what we narrated from the hadith of the two Sahihs is a specific incident and not general to all parts of the day. It is possible that the permission to fast in that incident was due to the presence of intention in most of it, or it is possible that it was for absolute permission during the day. As will come regarding invalidating by inserting something like iron. If the author had said, as in al-Fath, 'It is refraining from intercourse and from entering anything into the stomach or what has the ruling of the internal, from dawn to sunset, with intention', it would have been better.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion (sharh) of the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author first defines fasting (sawm) in Sacred Law as intentional abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from true dawn until sunset. He clarifies that 'abstention' is an act of the responsible person (mukallaf) because obligations must be performable acts; hence, it means restraining oneself from nullifiers, not merely 'not doing' something. The intention (niyyah) is required to distinguish worship from habit. Fasting is specific to daytime because night is the normal time for eating. The commentator then explains conditions for the validity of fasting: being Muslim, free from menstruation/postpartum bleeding, etc. He then focuses on the timing of intention. For Ramadan, specified vow, and supererogatory fasts, intention may be made from after sunset (or after fajr according to some) until before half of the day (i.e., before zawal). This is a distinctive Hanafi ruling. The main dispute is with the Shafi'i school, which requires night intention (tabyit) for all obligatory fasts. The Hanafi proof is the hadith in Bukhari and Muslim: the Prophet commanded a man who had eaten in Ramadan to complete the rest of the day, and another who had not eaten to fast. This shows that intention after fajr is valid for a compulsory fast. The second hadith 'No fast for the one who does not intend it from the night' is interpreted by Hanafis as indicating perfection (kamal) rather than validity, or as specific to non-Ramadan obligatory fasts. The commentator refutes the Shafi'i interpretation by arguing that qiyas can specify a general hadith but cannot abrogate it, so the Ramadan fast is an exception. The Hanafi position is that intention is valid until before half the day, and for supererogatory fasts even later (with absolute intention). The passage ends by suggesting a more precise definition of fasting.

Key Terms
النية (al-niyyah): The mental determination to perform an act of worship. For fasting, its valid time is from night until before half the day.
التبييت (al-tabyit): Making the intention for fasting during the night (before fajr).
التخصيص (al-takhsis): The usul principle of specifying a general text (amm) with a specific text (khass).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the latest time to make intention for a Ramadan fast?
A) Before Fajr
B) Before half the day (zawal)
C) Before Maghrib
D) Before Asr

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows daytime intention for Ramadan until half the day, based on a specific hadith from the Sahihayn, and specifies the general hadith requiring night intention.

Does Ramadan Require Specific Intention? The Hanafi Defence
Inter-madhhab dispute on necessity of specific intention for Ramadan fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intention for Ramadan and explain why the school rejects the requirement of specific intention.

Core Idea
The time of Ramadan overrides any specific intention – the fast counts as Ramadan even if you intend something else.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rai'iq presents a classic inter-madhhab exchange between the Hanafis and the majority (especially Shafi'is) over whether the Ramadan fast requires a specific intention (niyyah al-ta'yin). Abu Hanifa held that any intention – absolute, nafl, or even another wajib – suffices because the temporal designation of Ramadan itself determines the obligation. The author defends this view with rational arguments, including an incisive analogy with Hajj.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested element
Determine the precise point of disagreement: whether a specific intention (e.g., 'I intend the Ramadan fast') is required for validity.
The Hanafi school allows any intention, while the Shafi'i school requires ta'yin.
2
State the Hanafi position
Articulate Abu Hanifa's ruling: the Ramadan fast is valid even if one intends a different type of fast (nafl, kaffarah) or has an absolute intention.
This is based on the 'nullity of the aspect' – the specific description is ignored because the time dominates.
3
State the opposing position
Identify the majority view (جمهور العلماء, especially Shafi'is): specific intention is necessary for the fast to count as Ramadan.
They argue that the intention determines the identity of the act.
4
Follow the Hanafi rational proof
Examine the key arguments: (1) negation of other acts does not create coercion; (2) the specific-through-general analogy only works if intended; (3) analogy with Hajj where Shafi'is accept transformation.
The author cites al-Tahrir to support the Hanafi position as 'the truth'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: if someone intends a nadhr fast during Ramadan, Hanafis count it as Ramadan; Shafi'is require qada of Ramadan.
This difference affects whether the missed Ramadan must be made up.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصح وهو فلم يشترط له نية التعيين فصح بنية صوم مباين له كالنفل والكفارات بناء على لغو الجهة التي عينها فيبقى الصوم المطلق، وبمطلق النية يصح صومه كالأخص نحو زيد يصاب بالأعم كيا إنسان، وجمهور العلماء على خلافه. قال في التحرير وهو الحق لأن نفي شرعية غيره إنما توجب صحته لو نواه ونفي صحة ما نواه من الغير لا يوجب وجود نية ما يصرح بقوله لم أرده بل لو ثبت لكان جبراً ولا جبر في العبادات. وقولهم الأخص يصاب بالأعم إنما يصح إذا أراد الأخص بالأعم ولو أراده لارتفع الخلاف، وأعجب من هذا ما روي عن زفر أن التعيين شرعاً يوجب الإصابة بلا نية ا.هـ. وقد يقال بأنه نوى أصل الصوم ووصفة والوقت لا يقبل الوصف فلغت نية الوصف وبقيت نية الأصل إذ ليس من ضرورة بطلان الوصف بطلان الأصل والإعراض إن ثبت فإنما هو في ضمن نية النفل أو القضاء وقد لغت بالاتفاق فيلغو ما في ضمنها، ولا يلزم الجبر لأن معنى القربة في أصل الصوم يتحقق لبقاء الاختيار للعبد فيه ولا يتحقق في الصفة إذ لا اختيار له فيها فلا يتصور منه إبدال هذا الوصف بوصف آخر في هذا الزمان فيسقط اعتبار نية الصفة فعلم أنه لا يلزم الجبر إلا لو قلنا بوقوع الصوم من غير نية أصلاً. وما ألزمنا به الشافعي هنا من لزوم الجبر لزمه في الحج فإنه صححه فرضاً بنية النفل فما هو جوابه فهو جوابنا .
It is valid, so he did not require an intention of specification, so it is valid with an intention of a type different from it, like nafl and kaffarat, based on the nullity of the aspect he specified, so the absolute fast remains. And with an absolute intention, his fast is valid, like the specific being achieved through the general, for example Zayd being hit by 'O man!' And the majority of scholars oppose him. He said in al-Tahrir: It is the truth because the negation of the legality of others only necessitates its validity if he intended it, and the negation of the validity of what he intended from others does not necessitate the existence of an intention of what he explicitly says 'I did not intend it'; rather, if it were established, it would be coercion, and there is no coercion in acts of worship. Their statement that the specific is achieved through the general is only true if he intended the specific by the general, and if he intended it, the dispute would be resolved. What is stranger than this is what was narrated from Zufr that specification in law necessitates achievement without intention. End. And it may be said that he intended the essence of the fast and its description, and the time does not accept the description, so the intention of the description becomes void and the intention of the essence remains, since it is not necessary that the invalidity of the description necessitates the invalidity of the essence. And if disinterest is established, it is only within the intention of nafl or qada, and it has been voided by consensus, so what is within it becomes void. And coercion does not follow because the meaning of devotion in the essence of the fast is achieved by the continuation of choice for the servant in it, but it is not achieved in the description since he has no choice in it, so it is not conceivable for him to replace this description with another description in this time, so the consideration of the intention of the description falls away. Thus it is known that coercion does not follow unless we say that the fast occurs without any intention at all. And what we criticized al-Shafi'i here regarding the necessity of coercion applies to him in Hajj, for he validates it as obligatory by intending nafl, so his answer is our answer.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the necessity of a specific intention (niyyah al-ta'yin) for the fast of Ramadan. The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa, is that the Ramadan fast is valid even if one intends a different type of fast (e.g., nafl or kaffarah) or has an absolute intention, because the time of Ramadan itself overrides the specific intention. The majority of scholars (جمهور العلماء), primarily the Shafi'is, require a specific intention for the fast to apply to Ramadan. The author, citing al-Tahrir, defends the Hanafi view with rational arguments: the negation of the legality of other acts does not create coercion (جبر) in worship; the example of the specific being achieved through the general is only applicable if one intended the specific; and the analogy with Hajj, where Shafi'is accept that intending nafl results in an obligatory Hajj, supports the Hanafi position. The passage also mentions the unique view of Zufr, who holds that the legal specification itself causes the fast to count even without intention, which is considered even more extreme.

Key Terms
نية التعيين (niyyah al-ta'yin): The intention to fast a specific day, e.g., intending 'tomorrow is Ramadan' rather than an absolute fast.
جبر (jabr): Coercion in worship—the idea that if an act is valid without proper intent, it becomes imposed against one's will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a person intends to fast a nadhr (vowed) fast during Ramadan?
A) The fast counts as the nadhr, and Ramadan must be made up.
B) The fast counts as Ramadan, and the nadhr is not fulfilled.
C) The fast is invalid, and both must be made up.
D) The fast is valid for neither; it becomes a nafl.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation of Ramadan dominates over the specific intention, making the fast valid regardless of what the individual intended, as long as an intention exists.

Timing of Intention Before Zawal
Ruling on the last permissible time for intention before midday

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the latest permissible time for making intention for fasting in a given locality based on the dawn share calculation.

Core Idea
Intention for fasting is valid before zawal only if the remaining time exceeds half the dawn period.

The Hanafi school establishes a precise condition for the validity of intention before midday: the remaining time until zawal must be greater than half the time from dawn (fajr) to zawal. This calculation depends on the length of the dawn period in each region, as demonstrated with examples from Egypt and Syria. Understanding this condition ensures that the intention is not made too late, preserving the fast's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dawn period
Determine the length of time from true dawn (fajr sadiq) to the moment of zawal (sun at zenith). This is called 'hissat al-fajr' (share of dawn).
The dawn period varies by latitude and season.
2
Calculate half of the dawn period
Halve the dawn period to find the threshold: if the remaining time to zawal is more than this half, intention is valid; if equal or less, it is not.
In the text, half of dawn share in Egypt is about 13 degrees, in Syria 14.5 degrees.
3
Compare remaining time to zawal
Subtract the current time from zawal. If this remaining time exceeds the half-dawn period, intention is valid. Even half a degree extra suffices.
4
Apply to your locality
Use prayer time schedules to find the dawn period and zawal time for your location, then compute the latest valid intention time.
This is a practical fatwa for muftis and fasting observers.

تنبيه : اعلم أن كل قطر نصف نهاره قبل زواله بقدر نصف حصة فجره فمتى كان الباقي للزوال أكثر من هذا النصف صح وإلا فلا. ففي مصر والشام تصح النية قبل الزوال بخمس عشرة درجة لوجود النية في أكثر النهار لأن نصف حصة الفجر لا تزيد على ثلاث عشرة درجة في مصر وأربع عشرة ونصف في الشام، فإذا كان الباقي إلى الزوال أكثر من نصف هذه الحصة ولو بنصف درجة صح الصوم. كذا حرره شيخ مشايخنا إبراهيم السائجاني رحمه الله تعالى .
Note: Know that in every region, the half of its day before its zawal is equal to half of the share of its dawn. So when the remaining time until zawal is more than this half, it is valid; otherwise not. In Egypt and Syria, the intention is valid fifteen degrees before zawal because the intention occurs in more than half the day, since half of the dawn share does not exceed thirteen degrees in Egypt and fourteen and a half in Syria. So if the remaining time until zawal is more than half of this share, even by half a degree, the fast is valid. So wrote the shaykh of our shaykhs, Ibrahim al-Saijani, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage provides a precise ruling on the latest time for making the intention for the Ramadan fast. According to the Hanafi school, the intention must be made before midday (zawal), but with a detailed condition: the intention is valid as long as the remaining time until zawal is more than half of the time from dawn (fajr) to the beginning of zawal. The author explains that this half is determined by the length of the dawn period (حصة الفجر) in each region. For example, in Egypt, half the dawn period is about 13 degrees, so the intention is valid up to 15 degrees before zawal (since 15 > 13). The ruling is attributed to Shaykh Ibrahim al-Saijani, indicating its practical application in later Hanafi fatwa.

Key Terms
حصة الفجر (hissat al-fajr): The time period from dawn to the beginning of zawal (midday).
زوال (zawal): The moment when the sun passes its zenith, i.e., midday.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Syria, half the dawn share is 14.5 degrees. What is the condition for intention to be valid before zawal?
A) The remaining time must be at least 14.5 degrees.
B) The remaining time must be more than 14.5 degrees.
C) The remaining time must be at most 14.5 degrees.
D) The remaining time must be exactly 15 degrees.

Key Takeaway
Intention for fasting is valid before zawal only if the remaining time strictly exceeds half the dawn period.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
