Rulings on Nadhr, Traveller, and Sick Intending Other Fasts
Detailed rulings on effect of intending other fasts for specific categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person who intends a nadhr, wajib, or nafl fast during Ramadan, distinguishing the positions for a traveller and a sick person based on the presence of concession (rukhsah).

Core Idea
Intending another fast during Ramadan: for nadhr, valid with qada; for traveller, wajib counts as intended; for sick, depends on harm.

This passage explores the effect of intending a different fast (nadhr, other wajib, or nafl) while in the month of Ramadan. The Hanafi school recognizes that the intention of Ramadan itself can be fulfilled even when the person intends a different obligation or voluntary fast, but the details vary depending on the person's status (healthy, traveller, or sick). The key factor is the presence of a legal concession (rukhsah) to break the fast, which affects whether the intended fast or Ramadan prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intending a non-Ramadan fast (nadhr, wajib, nafl) during a day of Ramadan. The ruling differs by category: for nadhr, the fast counts as Ramadan but qada of nadhr is required; for traveller, intending another wajib counts as that wajib; for nafl, more correct view is that it becomes Ramadan. For sick, multiple views.
The basic rule from Abu Hanifa is that any intention yields Ramadan, but specific circumstances modify the outcome.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is based on the existence of rukhsah (concession) for travellers and sick, and the principle that the time obligation (Ramadan) is stronger. The traveller has concession to break fast, so his intention of another wajib is valid and falls as that wajib because he is inclined to the lighter.
For nafl, because the reward in Ramadan is greater, it becomes Ramadan.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The specific reasoning: for traveller, the Lawgiver established concession, so his intention of another wajib is given effect. For nafl, the benefit (reward) is greater in Ramadan, so it defaults to Ramadan as if he had an absolute intention.
This is based on the principle that the more rewarding obligation takes precedence when intention is ambiguous.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Among Hanafis, there are multiple views on the sick person: some say he is like the traveller, others distinguish based on harm. A detailed view holds that if fasting harms him, he is like a traveller; if not, he is like a healthy person.
The detailed view is favored by later authorities like al-Kashf and Fath al-Qadir.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The detailed view (harm vs. no harm) is considered the most accurate by later scholars. This resolves the apparent contradiction between the views of Fakhr al-Islam and the author of al-Hidaya.
Al-Akmal criticized the detailed view, but the author of this passage defends it, clarifying its intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال صلاحيته لغيره من الصيام لكن بقي عليه إفادة صحة رمضان بنية واجب آخر، ويمكن أن يكون ذكر نية النفل إشارة إليه بجامع إلغاء الجهة لتعيينة. وإذا وقع عما نوى فهل يلزمه قضاء المنذور المعين لا ذكر لها في ظاهر الرواية، والأصح وجوب القضاء. كذا في الفتاوى الظهيرية. ولا يرد عليه المسافر فإنه لو نوى واجباً آخر في رمضان يصح عند أبي حنيفة ويقع عما نوى لإثبات الشارع الترخص له وهو في الميل إلى الأخف وهو في صوم الواجب المغاير لأنه في ذمته وفرض الوقت لا يكون في ذمته إلا إذا أدرك عدة من أيام أخر. وفي النفل عنه روايتان، أصحهما عدم صحة ما نوى ووقوعه عن فرض الوقت لأن فائدة النفل الثواب وهو في فرض الوقت أكثر كما لو أطلق النية. كذا في التقرير. فعلم بهذا أن المسافر يصح صومه عن رمضان بمطلق النية وبنية النفل على الأصح فيهما مع وجود الروايتين فيهما فلهذا لم يستثنه في المختصر، وأما المريض إذا نوى واجباً آخر أو نفلاً ففيه ثلاثة أقوال؛ فقيل يقع عن رمضان لأنه لما صام التحق بالصحيح واختاره فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة وجمع وصححه صاحب المجمع، وقيل يقع عما نوى كالمسافر واختاره صاحب الهداية وأكثر المشايخ، وقيل بأنه ظاهر الرواية، وينبغي أن يقع عن رمضان في النفل على الصحيح كالمسافر على ما قدمناه ، وقيل بالتفصيل بين أن يضره الصوم فتتعلق الرخصة بخوف الزيادة فيصير كالمسافر يقع عما نوى، وبين أن لا يضره الصوم كفساد الهضم فتتعلق الرخصة بحقيقته فيقع عن فرض الوقت واختاره صاحب الكشف وتبعه المحقق في فتح القدير والتحرير. وتعقبه الأكمل في التقرير بأن المعلوم أن المريض الذي لا يضره الصوم غير مرخص له. قوله : (ويمكن أن يكون ذكر نية النفل إشارة إليه (الخ قال في النهر فيه تدافع إذ بتقدير هذه الإشارة يكون النفل صفة كاشفة والصحة بالمغاير خاصة برمضان ولا دلالة في الكلام على اختصاص إصابة رمضان به وقوله الآتي فعلم بهذا الخ» يقتضي أن يكون قيداً فتديره والصواب أن يجعل قيداً. ولا دلالة في الكلام على إصابة رمضان بنية واجب آخر وإلى ذلك أشار الشارح بقوله «وكذا يجوز أيضاً صوم رمضان بنية واجب آخر وعبارته في الوافي بالمقصود مما هنا أو في حيث قال : وإن أطلق أو نوى واجباً آخر في غير نذر ونفل وسفر ويعلم منه الصحة فيما إذا نوى نفلاً بالأولى قوله : (وإذا وقع عما نوى إلى قوله كذا في الظهيرية يوجد في بعض النسخ والأنسب إسقاطه من هذا المحل لأن قوله ولا يرد عليه وفي بعض النسخ لئلا يرد عليه من متعلقات قوله ويمكن أن يكون الخ» قوله : (وتعقبه الأكمل الخ) أقول : يظهر لي أن ما فهمه الأكمل ليس مراداً للقائلين بالتفصيل بل مرادهم أن المريض تارة يضره الصوم بأن يصير الصوم سبباً لزيادة مرضه فهذا تتعلق الرخصة في حقه بخوف الزيادة فما دام يخافها يرخص له الفطر، ولا يمكن إلحاقه بالصحيح بل هو كالمسافر لوجود الرخصة . وتارة لا يضره الصوم وإنما حصل له من الضعف ما لا يقدر معه على أداء الصوم أصلاً فهذا تتعلق
It negates its validity for other fasts, but it remains that it establishes the validity of Ramadan by intending another wajib. It is possible that mentioning the intention of nafl indicates this due to the commonality of nullifying the aspect of specification. And if it falls as what he intended, is qada of the specified nadhr incumbent upon him? There is no mention of it in the zahir al-riwayah, and the correct view is the obligation of qada. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya. And the traveller is not an objection to this, for if he intends another wajib in Ramadan, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa and falls as what he intended because the Lawgiver established a concession for him, and he is inclined to the lighter, which is the different wajib fast because it is in his liability and the time's obligation is not in his liability unless he meets several other days. Regarding nafl, there are two narrations: the more correct is that what he intended is not valid and it falls as the time's obligation because the benefit of nafl is reward, and reward is more in the time's obligation as if he had an absolute intention. Thus in al-Taqrir. So it is known from this that the traveller's fast for Ramadan is valid with an absolute intention and with the intention of nafl according to the more correct of the two narrations in both, and therefore it was not excepted in al-Mukhtasar. As for the sick person, if he intends another wajib or nafl, there are three sayings: one says it falls as Ramadan because when he fasts he joins the healthy, chosen by Fakhr al-Islam, Shams al-A'imma, and others, and al-Majma' regarded as sound; another says it falls as what he intended like the traveller, chosen by the author of al-Hidaya and most shaykhs; a third says it is the zahir al-riwayah, and it should fall as Ramadan for nafl on the correct view like the traveller based on what we mentioned; and there is a detailed view: if fasting harms him, concession is based on fear of increase, so he becomes like a traveller and it falls as what he intended; if it does not harm him (e.g., indigestion), concession is based on its reality, so it falls as the time's obligation, chosen by the author of al-Kashf and followed by al-Muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tahrir. Al-Akmal in al-Taqrir criticized this, saying it is known that the sick person whom fasting does not harm is not granted concession. His statement: (And it is possible that mentioning the intention of nafl indicates it) – he said in al-Nahr that there is contradiction because on this assumption, nafl would be a descriptive quality, and validity by other than it is specific to Ramadan, and there is no indication in the speech to allocate the achieving of Ramadan with it. And his later statement 'thus it is known...' requires it to be a condition; so reflect, and the correct is to make it a condition. There is no indication in the speech of achieving Ramadan by intending another wajib, and to that the commentator pointed by his statement 'And likewise it is also permissible to fast Ramadan by intending another wajib' and his phrase in al-Wafi serves the purpose of what is here or where he said: if he makes an absolute intention or intends another wajib other than nadhr, nafl, and travel, and one knows from it the validity when he intends nafl a fortiori. His statement: (And if it falls as what he intended... up to 'thus in al-Zahiriyya') – exists in some copies, and it is more appropriate to drop it from this place because his statement 'and it is not objected' (in some copies 'lest it be objected') relates to the statement 'and it is possible that...'. His statement: (And al-Akmal criticized it) – I say: it appears to me that what al-Akmal understood is not the intended meaning of those who hold the detailed view. Rather, their intended meaning is that the sick person is sometimes harmed by fasting, such that fasting becomes a cause of increase of his illness; this concession relates to him due to fear of increase; as long as he fears it, breaking the fast is permitted for him, and he cannot be joined with the healthy; rather, he is like a traveller due to the existence of concession. And sometimes fasting does not harm him, but rather he gets such weakness that he cannot perform the fast at all; then the concession relates...
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on intention during Ramadan, focusing on special cases: the person with a vowed fast (nadhr), the traveller, and the sick person. It first addresses that if one intends a nadhr fast during Ramadan, the fast counts for Ramadan, but qada of the nadhr is required according to the correct view (as stated in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya). The traveller is distinguished: according to Abu Hanifa, if he intends another wajib fast, it falls as that wajib because he has a concession (rukhsah) to break the fast. For nafl intention, there are two narrations; the more correct is that it becomes Ramadan. The sick person has multiple opinions: some say his fast becomes Ramadan, others say it falls as what he intended like a traveller, some distinguish based on whether fasting harms him. The author cites al-Akmal's criticism of the detailed view and then defends it, clarifying that the detailed view distinguishes between harmful and non-harmful sickness. The final commentary clarifies the correct interpretation of earlier statements, emphasizing that the intention of nafl does not automatically indicate the ruling for other intentions.

Key Terms
المنذور المعين (al-mandhur al-mu'ayyan): A vowed fast specified for a particular day.
رخصة (rukhsah): A legal concession permitting an otherwise prohibited act due to necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The fast is invalid entirely.

Key Takeaway
The effect of intending another fast during Ramadan depends on the presence of a legal concession (rukhsah): for the healthy person with a nadhr, qada is required; for the traveller, the intended wajib counts; for the sick, harm determines the outcome.

Intending Another Fast in Ramadan: Rulings for Resident, Traveler, and Sick
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of intention in Ramadan fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each category (resident, traveler, sick) when a different fast is intended in Ramadan, and identify the primary intra-Hanafi disagreement on the sick person's case.

Core Idea
Intention can redirect Ramadan’s fast only when a legal concession (rukhsah) applies.

In Ramadan, the default is that fasting is for the month itself. But what if a fasting person intends a different obligatory fast or a voluntary fast? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq navigates the rulings for three categories: the resident (muqim), the traveler (musafir), and the sick (marid). The analysis reveals important distinctions based on the presence of concession (rukhsah) and the degree of harm. You will learn not only the soundest positions but also the internal Hanafi disagreements, especially regarding the sick person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories and the base principle
Recognize that the passage addresses three statuses: resident (muqim), traveler (musafir), and sick (marid). The core issue is whether the fast of Ramadan can serve a different obligation or nafl based on intention.
2
Analyze the resident case and the soundest opinion on qada'
For a resident who intends a specified vowed fast (manthur mu'ayyan) in Ramadan, the soundest opinion (al-ashah) is that he must make up (qada') that vowed fast. This ruling is from later fatwa works, not Zahir al-Riwayah.
The resident does not have a concession, so the intention does not redirect; he must fulfill the vow separately.
3
Examine the traveler's concession and its effect
For the traveler, the Lawgiver granted a concession (rukhsah). Therefore, if he intends another wajib fast, it falls on that intention. For nafl, the more sound narration is that it counts for Ramadan, not the nafl.
The traveler's fast is valid for Ramadan even with an absolute intention, due to the concession.
4
Map the three opinions on the sick person and the tarjih
For the sick person who intends another wajib or nafl, there are three opinions: (1) it falls for Ramadan (Fakhr al-Islam et al.), (2) it falls for what he intended like the traveler (majority of mashayikh), and (3) a detailed view distinguishing harmful vs non-harmful illness. The majority opinion (from Sahib al-Hidayah) is preferred, but the detailed view is also considered.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the sick intends a nafl, the first view gives reward for Ramadan, the second requires qada' of Ramadan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال صلاحيته لغيره من الصيام لكن بقي عليه إفادة صحة رمضان بنية واجب آخر، ويمكن أن يكون ذكر نية النفل إشارة إليه بجامع إلغاء الجهة لتعيينة. وإذا وقع عما نوى فهل يلزمه قضاء المنذور المعين لا ذكر لها في ظاهر الرواية، والأصح وجوب القضاء. كذا في الفتاوى الظهيرية. ولا يرد عليه المسافر فإنه لو نوى واجباً آخر في رمضان يصح عند أبي حنيفة ويقع عما نوى لإثبات الشارع الترخص له وهو في الميل إلى الأخف وهو في صوم الواجب المغاير لأنه في ذمته وفرض الوقت لا يكون في ذمته إلا إذا أدرك عدة من أيام أخر. وفي النفل عنه روايتان، أصحهما عدم صحة ما نوى ووقوعه عن فرض الوقت لأن فائدة النفل الثواب وهو في فرض الوقت أكثر كما لو أطلق النية. كذا في التقرير. فعلم بهذا أن المسافر يصح صومه عن رمضان بمطلق النية وبنية النفل على الأصح فيهما مع وجود الروايتين فيهما فلهذا لم يستثنه في المختصر، وأما المريض إذا نوى واجباً آخر أو نفلاً ففيه ثلاثة أقوال؛ فقيل يقع عن رمضان لأنه لما صام التحق بالصحيح واختاره فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة وجمع وصححه صاحب المجمع، وقيل يقع عما نوى كالمسافر واختاره صاحب الهداية وأكثر المشايخ، وقيل بأنه ظاهر الرواية، وينبغي أن يقع عن رمضان في النفل على الصحيح كالمسافر على ما قدمناه ، وقيل بالتفصيل بين أن يضره الصوم فتتعلق الرخصة بخوف الزيادة فيصير كالمسافر يقع عما نوى، وبين أن لا يضره الصوم كفساد الهضم فتتعلق الرخصة بحقيقته فيقع عن فرض الوقت واختاره صاحب الكشف وتبعه المحقق في فتح القدير والتحرير. وتتعقبه الأكمل في التقرير بأن المعلوم أن المريض الذي لا يضره الصوم غير مرخص له
It invalidates its suitability for other fasts, but it remains that it gives validity to Ramadan with the intention of another obligatory act. And it is possible that mentioning the intention of nafl is an indication of that, by the common factor of disregarding the category for its specification. And if it falls on what he intended, then is he required to make up the specified vowed fast? There is no mention of it in Zahir al-Riwayah, and the soundest opinion is that qada' is obligatory. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah. And the case of the traveler does not refute it, for if he intended another obligatory act in Ramadan, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa and it falls on what he intended, because the Lawgiver established a concession for him, and he is inclined to the lighter, and it is concerning the fast of a different obligatory act because it is in his liability, and the duty of the time is not in his liability unless he reaches several other days. And concerning nafl from him, there are two narrations; the more sound of them is the unsoundness of what he intended and its falling for the duty of the time, because the benefit of nafl is reward, and it is more in the duty of the time, just as if he made an absolute intention. Thus in al-Taqrir. So it is known from this that the traveler's fast for Ramadan is valid with an absolute intention and with intention of nafl, according to the more sound in both, despite the existence of two narrations in them; therefore he did not exempt it in al-Mukhtasar. As for the sick person, if he intended another obligatory act or a nafl, then there are three sayings: it is said it falls for Ramadan because when he fasted he joined the healthy, chosen by Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma and a group, and corrected by the author of al-Majma'; and it is said it falls for what he intended like the traveler, chosen by the author of al-Hidayah and most of the mashayikh; and it is said it is the apparent of the narration; and it should be that it falls for Ramadan in nafl on the sound view like the traveler according to what we have presented; and it is said with detail between when the fast harms him, then the concession is attached to fear of increase, so he becomes like the traveler, it falls for what he intended; and when it does not harm him like corruption of digestion, then the concession is attached to the reality of the illness, so it falls for the duty of the time, chosen by the author of al-Kashf, followed by the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tahrir. And al-Akmal criticized it in al-Taqrir by [saying] that it is known that the sick person whom fasting does not harm is not granted a concession.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the intricate rulings of intention (niyyah) in Ramadan fasting when one intends a different obligatory fast or a voluntary fast. The commentary opens by establishing that if a resident (muqim) intends a specified vowed fast (manthur mu'ayyan) in Ramadan, and the fast falls on that intended vow, the soundest opinion (al-ashah) is that qada' is obligatory, even though there is no explicit mention in Zahir al-Riwayah. This is from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah. The author then distinguishes the traveler (musafir): if he intends another obligatory fast, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa and falls on what he intended, because the Lawgiver gave him a concession (rukhas) to incline to the lighter. For nafl in a traveler, there are two narrations; the more sound is that he does not fulfil the nafl, rather the fast counts for Ramadan. This is because the reward of Ramadan exceeds that of nafl. After detailing the traveler's case, the author presents three opinions for the sick person (marid) who intends another wajib or nafl. First opinion: it falls for Ramadan because fasting joins him to the healthy. Second: it falls for what he intended like the traveler. Third: a detailed view distinguishing between harmful and non-harmful fasting conditions. The author notes that al-Akmal refuted this detail, asserting that a sick person not harmed by fasting has no concession. This passage is a classic example of Hanafi legal analysis applying principles of intention, concession, and the effect of different statuses (muqim, musafir, marid) on the validity of fasting.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform a specific act of worship; required for validity of fasting.
منذور معين (manthur mu'ayyan): A specific vowed fast that one has obligated upon oneself, e.g., 'I will fast on Monday.'
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad in his six authoritative books.
رخصة (rukhsah): A legal concession granted due to a valid excuse, such as travel or illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the soundest opinion (al-ashah) regarding a resident who intends a specified vowed fast (manthur mu'ayyan) in Ramadan?
A) The fast counts for the vow, and he must make up Ramadan later.
B) The fast counts for Ramadan, and there is nothing owed.
C) The fast is invalid and must be repeated.
D) He must make up both the vow and the Ramadan day.

Key Takeaway
The effect of intention in Ramadan fasting depends on the presence of a legal concession (rukhsah): only those with a concession (traveler, sick with harm) can redirect the fast to another obligation; otherwise it remains for Ramadan.

The Principle of Separation of Origin and Description: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Exposition of Abu Hanifa's principle and internal Hanafi disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's principle that the original separates from the description and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, using examples from prayer, fasting, and divorce.

Core Idea
The original act can survive an invalid description; intention does not change the essence.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author presents a fundamental legal principle of Abu Hanifa: the original (asl) of a legal act is separable from its description (wasf). This principle has significant implications across various chapters of fiqh, particularly in prayer, fasting, and divorce. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagreed, arguing that for matters that do not accept 'indication' (isharah), the description is inseparable. Their disagreement led to a sophisticated intra-madhhab debate that was later refined by commentators like al-Akmal.

Guided Path
1
Identify Abu Hanifa's principle
State the principle that the original separates from the description. Understand that the legal act remains valid even if the intended description (e.g., obligatory or voluntary) is invalidated.
This is the core qawida.
2
List the three applications given
Work through the examples: prayer (invalid description does not invalidate the prayer), fasting (invalid description does not invalidate the fast), and delegated divorce (the original divorce occurs, description delegated to wife).
Each example illustrates the same principle.
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argued that for matters that do not accept isharah (like fasting and prayer), the description is inseparable. Note their partial agreements: Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa on prayer, Muhammad disagrees on both.
This shows the nuance within the school.
4
Analyze al-Akmal's refutation
Al-Akmal uses riba and invalid contracts as a counterexample. If the Sahibayn's principle were applied, riba would either be void (if original follows description, which is invalid) or valid (if description follows original, which is valid), both of which are false by consensus.
This refutation strengthens Abu Hanifa's principle.
5
Apply the principle to a new case
Consider a person who intends a nafl fast during Ramadan. According to Abu Hanifa, the fast counts for Ramadan. According to the Sahibayn, it might count for the intended nafl or be invalid. Conclude which is mufta bihi.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the practical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن أبا حنيفة جرى على أصله في المواضع كلها من أن الأصل ينفك عن الوصف فلهذا قال : إذا بطلت صفة الفرضية في الصلاة لا يبطل أصلها، وإذا بطلت الصفة في الصوم بقي أصله، وإذا قال لها أنت طالق كيف شئت وقع أصل الطلاق وكان الوصف مفوضاً إليها. وهما قالا في هذه المسألة بأن ما لا يقبل الإشارة من الأمور الشرعية فحاله ووصفه بمنزلة أصله فيتعلق الأصل بتعلقه فخالفا هذا الأصل في الصوم وخالفه أبو يوسف في الصلاة لأنه موافق لأبي حنيفة فيها وجرى عليه محمد في الصلاة فإنه قال ببطلان الأصل إذا بطل الوصف فيها، وقد فرق بعضهم لمحمد بين الصوم والصلاة ورده الأكمل في تقريره وقال في بحث : كيف أن أصلهما المذكور ليس بصحيح لأن صحته تستلزم انتفاء الفاسد على مذهبنا واللازم باطل لأن الأحكام عندنا تنقسم إلى جائز وفاسد وباطل بيان الملازمة أن الربا مثلاً وسائر العقودات الفاسدة مشروعة بأصلها غير مشروعة بوصفها بالاتفاق وهي مما لا يقبل الإشارة. فلو كان ما ذكرناه صحيحاً لكان الأصل فيه مثل الوصف والوصف غير مشروع، وما كان غير مشروع بحسب الأصل والوصف فهو باطل اتفاقاً لا فاسد أو كان الوصف مثل الأصل والأصل مشروع فكان الربا جائزاً لا فاسداً . وهو باطل إجماعاً ا اهـ.
Then know that Abu Hanifa followed his principle in all places, that the original separates from the description. For this he said: If the attribute of obligatory nature is invalidated in prayer, its original does not become invalid; and if the attribute is invalidated in fasting, its original remains; and if he says to her 'You are divorced however you wish,' the original of divorce occurs and the description is delegated to her. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said in this issue that whatever does not accept indication from among the legal matters, its state and description are like its original, so the original is attached to its attachment. So they contradicted this principle in fasting, and Abu Yusuf contradicted him in prayer because he agreed with Abu Hanifa in it, and Muhammad followed him in prayer, for he said that the original becomes invalid if the description is invalidated in it. And some made a distinction for Muhammad between fasting and prayer, but al-Akmal refuted it in his Taqrir and said in a discussion: How? That their mentioned principle is not correct because its correctness necessitates the negation of the invalid according to our madhhab, and the necessary is false because rulings according to us are divided into valid, invalid, and void. The explanation of the implication is that riba, for example, and all invalid contracts are legislated in their original but not legislated in their description by consensus, and they are among things that do not accept indication. So if what we mentioned were correct, then the original in them would be like the description, and the description is not legislated; and what is not legislated in terms of original and description is void by consensus, not invalid; or the description would be like the original and the original is legislated, so riba would be valid, not invalid. And it is void by consensus, end.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a fundamental legal principle (qawida) of Abu Hanifa: the original legal act (asl) can be separated from its description (wasf). Several applications are given: if the attribute of obligatory nature (wasf al-fardiyya) is invalidated in prayer, the prayer itself remains (e.g., if one intended a nafl but the time was for fajr, the prayer is still valid as nafl). Similarly in fasting, if the intended description fails, the fast remains as Ramadan. In divorce, if a man says 'You are divorced however you wish,' the divorce takes effect originally and the description (e.g., number of divorces) is delegated to the wife. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) differed, arguing that for matters that do not accept 'indication' (isharah), the state (hal) and description are inseparable from the original. They applied this in fasting (contradicting Abu Hanifa) but Abu Yusuf agreed with Abu Hanifa in prayer, while Muhammad disagreed in prayer, saying the original is null. The commentator mentions a distinction drawn for Muhammad between fasting and prayer, but al-Akmal refutes this by using the example of riba: invalid contracts like usury have a valid original but invalid description by consensus; if the Sahibayn's principle were applied, riba would become either void entirely or valid entirely, which is false. Thus the principle of separation is upheld. This is a sophisticated usul al-fiqh discussion within a fiqh text.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-asl): The original legal act or entity considered independently of its attributes.
الوصف (al-wasf): The legal description or attribute attached to the original, such as obligatory or voluntary.
ما لا يقبل الإشارة (ma la yaqbal al-isharah): Matters that do not accept indication from the Law; i.e., their essence is fixed and cannot be changed by intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa's principle, if a person intends a nafl fast during Ramadan, what happens?
A) The fast is invalid entirely.
B) The fast counts for the nafl.
C) The fast counts for Ramadan.
D) The fast counts for both.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's principle that the original separates from the description is the relied-upon view, applicable to prayer, fasting, and divorce unless consensus indicates otherwise.

Requirement of Specific Intention from Night for Other Fasts
Ruling on fasts other than Ramadan requiring specific night intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the fasts that require a specific intention made during the night and explain why specification and tabyit are necessary for their validity.

Core Idea
Fasts other than Ramadan require both a specific intention and an intention made during the night.

This lesson focuses on a key principle from Kanz al-Daqaiq: for certain fasts—such as qada of Ramadan, expiations, and vows—a general intention is insufficient. You must specify the type of fast and make the intention before dawn. Understanding this distinction is crucial for ensuring the validity of these acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in the matn
The matn states: 'And what remains is not permissible except with a specific intention made at night.' This is the core ruling.
The matn is authoritative from Kanz al-Daqaiq.
2
List the fasts covered by the ruling
The commentator explains that 'what remains' includes: qada of Ramadan, kaffarat (expiations), jaza' al-sayd (penalty for hunting in ihram), halq (shaving during hajj), tamattu' (compensation for tamattu' hajj), and al-nadhr al-mutlaq (absolute vow).
These are obligatory fasts with unspecified times.
3
Understand the two requirements: specification and night intention
Two conditions are necessary: (1) ta'yin (specification) – you must intend which specific fast you are keeping, e.g., 'I intend to fast tomorrow for qada of Ramadan.' (2) tabyit (night intention) – the intention must be made during the night before fajr.
Without both, the fast is invalid.
4
Distinguish from Ramadan
Ramadan is excluded from this ruling. For Ramadan, a general intention (e.g., 'I will fast tomorrow') suffices, and the intention can be made after dawn if needed, according to the Hanafi school.
This is because Ramadan's time is specified, so a general intention covers it.
5
Note the concession for the sick
The passage briefly touches on a concession: if a person is ill such that they cannot fast at all, they are excused. Once they are able to fast, the concession ends and they must comply with the normal requirements.
This part is cut off, but the main point is that the ruling applies to those capable of fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما بقي لم يجز إلا بنية معينة مبيتة) أي ما بقي من الصيام وهو قضاء رمضان والكفارات وجزاء الصيد والحلق والمتعة والنذر المطلق لا يصح بمطلق النية ولا بنية مباينة، ولا بد فيه من التعيين لعدم تعين الوقت له ولا بد فيه أيضاً من النية من الليل أو ما هو في الرخصة في حقه بحقيق المرض أي ما دام هذا المرض الذي لا يمكنه معه ، الصوم أصلاً يرخص له فقد زال المرخص فصار كالصحيح لا كالمسافر. والحاصل أن المرض الفطر، فإذا قدر على الع . سوم
His saying: (And what remains is not permissible except with a specific intention made at night) i.e., what remains of fasts, and they are: making up Ramadan, expiations, penalty for hunting, shaving, tamattu', and absolute vow, are not valid with an absolute intention nor with a divergent intention, and specification is necessary due to the time not being specified for them, and also intention from the night is necessary, or what is in the concession in his right due to the reality of illness, i.e., as long as this illness that he cannot fast at all, concession is granted to him; then the conceser is removed, so he becomes like the healthy, not like the traveler. The conclusion is that illness breaks the fast, and if he is able to...[text breaks]
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage states a key ruling from the matn: 'And what remains is not permissible except with a specific intention made at night.' The commentator explains that 'what remains' refers to fasts other than Ramadan: qada of Ramadan, kaffarat (expiation for breaking a fast), the penalty for hunting (jaza' al-sayd), shaving (al-halq), tamattu' (compensation for tamattu' hajj), and absolute vows (nadhr mutlaq). These are not valid with a general intention (mutlaq al-niyyah) nor with an intention that is different (mubayyinah, e.g., intending a different obligatory fast). They require specification (ta'yin) because the time is not designated for them, and they require intention from the night (tabyit). The commentary then discusses the concession for the sick: if the illness is such that one cannot fast at all, concession is given, but once able, the concession ends and the person is like a healthy person, not a traveler. The text ends abruptly, but the main ruling is clearly presented.

Key Terms
نية معينة مبيتة (niyyah mu'ayyanah mubayyatah): A specific intention made during the night before dawn; required for certain fasts other than Ramadan.
النذر المطلق (al-nadhr al-mutlaq): A vow that is not specified to a particular time, e.g., 'I will fast for Allah.'
جزاء الصيد (jaza' al-sayd): The penalty for hunting in the state of ihram, which may involve fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Fasts other than Ramadan require both specification (ta'yin) and night intention (tabyit); without either, the fast is invalid.

Intention for Voluntary and Make-up Fasts: Conditions
Ruling on conditions of intention for non-Ramadan fasts with exception for Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions (ta'yin, tabyit) for a valid non-Ramadan fast and apply them to qada, kaffarat, and nafal, and explain the ruling when one intends nafal on a day that turns out to be Ramadan.

Core Idea
Non-Ramadan fasts require specific and pre-dawn intention; error is excused if due to sound doubt.

This lesson examines the conditions for fasting outside Ramadan. The Hanafi school holds that any fast other than the obligatory Ramadan fast requires a specific intention (ta'yin) made during the night (tabyit). The passage also addresses a subtle case: if someone intends a voluntary fast but later discovers it is Ramadan, the fast is valid and counts for Ramadan, as long as the error was based on sound doubt. This ruling is contrasted with the Shafi'i position, which considers such error as potentially leading to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts covered
The phrase 'what remains' (ma baqiya) includes qada of Ramadan, expiations (kaffarat), compensation for hunting/shaving, tamattu', and absolute vows. Note which fasts fall under this rule.
These are contrasted with the fixed Ramadan fast, which does not require a new intention each day.
2
State the two conditions
For each of these fasts, two conditions must be met: (1) ta'yin – specifying the type of fast, and (2) tabyit – making the intention during the night, before dawn.
A mere general intention to fast is insufficient; the exact type must be specified.
3
Apply to a scenario: intending nafal on Ramadan day
If someone intends a voluntary fast at night and later learns it is Ramadan, the fast is valid and counts for Ramadan, provided the error was based on sound doubt (e.g., unclear sighting). If the error was reckless, kufr is feared.
This is the Hanafi position; Shafi'is hold that any intentional belief that a Ramadan day is nafal invalidates the fast and can cause kufr.
4
Review the branch: daytime intention for qada
If someone intends to make up a missed Ramadan fast during the day, the qada is invalid. According to Fatawa al-Nasafi, it may count as a voluntary fast if so intended, but the qada obligation remains.
This shows the strictness of tabyit for non-Ramadan fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الفرضية أو طنه فقد يكون معتقداً للفرضية ذلك نوى النفل فلا يكون بنية النفل كافراً إلا إذا انضم إليها اعتقاد النفلية، وكذا لا يخشى علبه الكفر إلا إذا انضم إليها الظن المذكور والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم . وقع أصل الطلاق وكان الوصف مفوضاً إليها. وهما قالا في هذه المسألة بأن ما لا يقبل الإشارة من الأمور الشرعية فحاله ووصفه بمنزلة أصله فيتعلق الأصل بتعلقه فخالفا هذا الأصل في الصوم وخالفه أبو يوسف في الصلاة لأنه موافق لأبي حنيفة فيها وجرى عليه محمد في الصلاة فإنه قال ببطلان الأصل إذا بطل الوصف فيها، وقد فرق بعضهم لمحمد بين الصوم والصلاة ورده الأكمل في تقريره وقال في بحث : كيف أن أصلهما المذكور ليس بصحيح لأن صحته تستلزم انتفاء الفاسد على مذهبنا واللازم باطل لأن الأحكام عندنا تنقسم إلى جائز وفاسد وباطل بيان الملازمة أن الربا مثلاً وسائر العقودات الفاسدة مشروعة بأصلها غير مشروعة بوصفها بالاتفاق التقديم للضرورة ومن فروع لزوم التبييت في غير المعين لو نوى القضاء نهاراً فلم يصح هل يقع عن النفل في فتاوى النسفي نعم ولو أفطر يلزمه القضاء. قيل هذا إذا علم أن صومه عن لم يصح بنية من النهار، وأما إذا في الليل على الفطر لم يصبح صائماً، فلو أفطر لا شيء عليه إن لم يكن رمضان ولو مضى عليه لا يجزئه لأن تلك النية انتقضت بالرجوع ولو نوى الصائم الفطر لم يفطر حتى يأكل، وكذا لو نوى التكلم في الصلاة كذا في الظهيرية ولو قال نويت صوم غد إن شاء الله تعالى؛ فعن الحلواني يجوز استحساناً لأن المشيئة إنما تبطل اللفظ والنية فعل القلب وصححه في فتاوى الظهيرية
And the intention for voluntary (fasts) and what remains is not valid except with a specific intention made at night. Ramadan is established by sighting its crescent or after completing thirty days of Sha'ban for the resident... So whoever is not harmed by fasting is correct, and the discussion is not about that. Then know that it occurred in the statements of the scholars, both in principles and branches, that Ramadan is valid despite an error in description. So a group of mashayikh held that the issue of intending voluntary fasting in Ramadan is from the sound doubt: that one begins with this intention, then it appears that it is Ramadan, so it is excused. But if it occurs in other than that, one fears for him kufr because he thought the command for specific abstention is fulfilled by something else. In al-Taqrir it is mentioned. And in al-Nihaya it is refuted, as he said in the proof of al-Shafi'i: if one believes the prescribed act at this time to be voluntary, he becomes a disbeliever. And he said in its refutation that since voluntary sufficed him, the intention of aversion is not realized; thus his saying that if he believes it to be voluntary he becomes a disbeliever is invalidated. The conclusion: there is no necessary connection between intending voluntary and belief in non-obligation or its doubt; one may believe it obligatory yet intend voluntary, so he is not a disbeliever by intending voluntary unless accompanied by belief of voluntariness. Also, kufr is not feared unless accompanied by that doubt. Then know that Abu Hanifa followed his principle in all places that the original is separate from the description; thus he said: if the quality of obligation is invalidated in prayer, its original is not invalidated; if the quality is invalidated in fasting, its original remains; and if he says to her 'you are divorced as you wish', the original divorce occurs and the description is delegated to her. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said in this issue that what does not admit of pointing from among shari'i matters, its state and description are like its original, so the original is attached to its attachment. Thus they opposed this principle in fasting, and Abu Yusuf opposed him in prayer because he agreed with Abu Hanifa in it, and Muhammad acted on it in prayer, for he said the original is invalidated if the description is invalidated. Some differentiated for Muhammad between fasting and prayer, but al-Akmal refuted it in his Taqrir and said in his discussion: how is it that their mentioned principle is not sound because its soundness necessitates the absence of the fasid according to our madhhab, and the consequent is false because rulings according to us are divided into ja'iz, fasid, and batil. The demonstration: usury and all fasid contracts are legislated in their origin, not legislated in their description by agreement, and they are what do not admit of pointing. So if what we mentioned were sound, the origin would be like the description, and the description is non-legislated; what is non-legislated in both origin and description is batil by agreement, not fasid; or the description would be like the origin and the origin is legislated, so usury would be ja'iz, not fasid. That is invalid by consensus. His statement: 'and what remains is not valid except with a specific intention made at night' means what remains of fasting, which is make-up of Ramadan, expiations, compensation for hunting, shaving, tamattu', and absolute vows. It is not valid with absolute intention nor with contrary intention; specification is necessary because its time is not designated, and intention from the night or what is in its ruling is also necessary, which is being concomitant with the break of dawn; rather it is the original because the obligation is to attach intention to fasting, not to advance it; advancement is permitted only for necessity. Among the branches of the necessity of tabyit for non-designated: if one intends make-up during the day, it is not valid; does it fall as voluntary? In Fatawa al-Nasafi: yes, and if he breaks his fast, he must make it up. It is said: this is if he knows that his fast is not valid with daytime intention; but if he does not know, he is not bound by the commencement as in the doubtful. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and what appears is preferring the absolute, because ignorance of rulings in Dar al-Islam is not considered, especially since this issue—i.e., the impermissibility of make-up with daytime intention—is agreed upon apparently, so it is not like the doubtful. And it is not hidden that the make-up of voluntary after invalidating it and the make-up of designated vows are included under his statement 'what remains'. Then know that intention from the night is sufficient for every fast provided there is no revocation; so if one intends at night to fast tomorrow, then resolves at night to break it, he does not become a faster; if he breaks it, nothing is upon him if it is not Ramadan, and if the day passes, it does not suffice him because that intention was revoked by withdrawal. If the faster intends to break, he does not break until he eats; similarly if one intends to speak in prayer—so in al-Zahiriyya. If one says 'I intend to fast tomorrow if Allah wills', from al-Halwani: it is permissible by istihsan because the conditional only invalidates the wording, and intention is an act of the heart; and it was authenticated in Fatawa al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion probe on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the conditions for intention in fasting other than Ramadan. The author begins by stating the core ruling: voluntary fasts and what remains (i.e., make-up fasts, expiations, etc.) require a specific intention (ta'yin) made during the night (tabyit). He then addresses a subtle issue: if someone starts a fast in Ramadan thinking it is voluntary, but it later becomes clear it is Ramadan, the fast is valid and the person is excused due to sound doubt (shakk sahih). However, if someone believes a prescribed act is voluntary in another context, that could lead to kufr because it implies rejection of the obligation. The commentator then enters a deep discussion on a principle of Abu Hanifa: the original act (asl) can be separated from its description (wasf). So if the description of obligation is invalid in prayer, the prayer itself remains valid; similarly in fasting. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differed: they held that in matters not subject to pointing, the description is like the origin, so invalidating the description invalidates the origin. This leads to intra-madhhab disagreements on specific cases. The commentator refutes the principle of the two companions by using the example of riba and fasid contracts, concluding that the principle is unsound. Returning to the matn, the author explains that 'what remains' includes qada of Ramadan, kaffarat, fidyah for hunting, shaving, tamattu', and absolute vows. For these, both specification and pre-dawn intention are mandatory. A branch: if someone intends qada during the day, it is invalid; according to Fatawa al-Nasafi, it would count as voluntary, but he must still make up the qada later. The passage concludes with fine points about the effect of revocation of intention and the ruling on conditional intention (e.g., 'if Allah wills').

Key Terms
نية النفل (niyyat al-nafl): The intention to perform a voluntary (supererogatory) fast.
ما بقي (ma baqiya): Fasts other than the obligatory Ramadan fast, including make-up (qada), expiations (kaffarat), and other obligatory fasts.
تبييت (tabyit): Making the intention during the night (before dawn) for the coming day's fast.
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyyah): Specifying the type of fast (e.g., qada of Ramadan, expiation) in the intention.
الوصف (al-wasf): The description or quality of the act (e.g., obligatory, voluntary) as distinct from the act itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required for a make-up (qada) fast to be valid?
A) Any intention to fast is sufficient.
B) A specific intention to make up Ramadan and a pre-dawn intention.
C) Only a pre-dawn intention, without specifying the type.
D) An intention after dawn is acceptable if made before noon.

Key Takeaway
Every fast outside Ramadan requires a specific and pre-dawn intention; sound doubt excuses error in description.

Sawm — Fasting
advanced
10 min
Separation of Original and Description: Abu Hanifa's Principle
Intra-Hanafi dispute on separation of original and description with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu Hanifa's principle that the original act (asl) is separate from its description (wasf), and apply it to the dispute with the two companions regarding fasting and prayer.

Core Idea
Invalidating the description does not invalidate the original act according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson delves into a key usul principle that underlies many rulings in Hanafi fiqh: the original act (asl) can exist independently of its description (wasf). The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents Abu Hanifa's position that if the quality of obligation is invalidated in acts like prayer or fasting, the act itself remains valid. The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, disagree, holding that in certain matters the description is inseparable from the origin. The commentator refutes their principle using the example of riba and fasid contracts, demonstrating that it would lead to absurd conclusions.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's principle
The principle: 'الأصل ينفك عن الوصف' – the original is separate from the description. Thus, invalidating the description (e.g., thinking a fast is nafal when it is Ramadan) does not invalidate the original act of fasting.
This allows for a valid act despite error in intention quality.
2
Identify the opposing view of the two companions
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say: In matters that do not admit of pointing (i.e., non-tangible actions), the state and description are like the origin. So if the description is invalid, the original is also invalid.
This leads them to say that if the quality of obligation is missing, the entire act is void.
3
Trace the application to fasting and prayer
Abu Hanifa: If the 'obligatory' quality is missing, the fast or prayer remains valid (as nafal). The two companions: The entire act becomes invalid. Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa in prayer; Muhammad disagrees in prayer.
This shows internal ikhtilaf.
4
Examine the refutation using riba
The commentator argues: If the companions' principle were correct, then fasid contracts like riba would be ja'iz (valid), because their origin is legislated, and if description equals origin, the whole contract would be valid. This is false by consensus.
This qiyas shows the unsoundness of the companions' principle.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
Abu Hanifa's view is relied upon. The separation principle applies, ensuring that minor errors in intention quality do not invalidate the entire act.
This has practical implications for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونية النفل وما بقي لم يجز إلا بنية معينة مبيتة ويثبت رمضان برؤية هلاله أو بعد شعبان المقيم إنما ومع الفطر عند أئمة الفقه كما شهدت كتبهم بذلك فمن لا يضره الصوم صحيح وليس الكلام فيه. ثم اعلم أنه وقع في عبارة القوم أصولاً وفروعاً أن رمضان يصح مع الخطأ في الوصف، فذهب جماعة من المشايخ إلى أن مسألة نية الصوم النفل في رمضان من الصحيح الشك بأن شرع بهذه النية ثم ظهر أنه من رمضان حتى يكون مصورة في يوم هي هذا الظن معفواً، فأما لو وجدت في غيره يخشى عليه الكفر لأنه ظن أن الأمر بالإمساك المعين يتأدى بغيره وبمثل هذ الظن يخشى عليه الكفر . كذا في التقرير. وفي النهاية ما يرده إنه قال في دليل الشافعي : إنه لو اعتقد المشروع في هذا الوقت أنه نفل يكفر. وقال في رده إنه لما اغانية النفل لم تتحقق نية الإعراض وبه يبطل قوله أنه لو اعتقد فيه أنه نفل يكفر ا هـ . والحاصل أنه لا ملازمة بين نية النفل واعتقاد عدم الفرضية أو طنه فقد يكون معتقداً للفرضية ذلك نوى النفل فلا يكون بنية النفل كافراً إلا إذا انضم إليها اعتقاد النفلية، وكذا لا يخشى علبه الكفر إلا إذا انضم إليها الظن المذكور والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم . ثم اعلم أن أبا حنيفة جرى على أصله في المواضع كلها من أن الأصل ينفك عن الوصف فلهذا قال : إذا بطلت صفة الفرضية في الصلاة لا يبطل أصلها، وإذا بطلت الصفة في الصوم بقي أصله، وإذا قال لها أنت طالق كيف شئت وقع أصل الطلاق وكان الوصف مفوضاً إليها. وهما قالا في هذه المسألة بأن ما لا يقبل الإشارة من الأمور الشرعية فحاله ووصفه بمنزلة أصله فيتعلق الأصل بتعلقه فخالفا هذا الأصل في الصوم وخالفه أبو يوسف في الصلاة لأنه موافق لأبي حنيفة فيها وجرى عليه محمد في الصلاة فإنه قال ببطلان الأصل إذا بطل الوصف فيها، وقد فرق بعضهم لمحمد بين الصوم والصلاة ورده الأكمل في تقريره وقال في بحث : كيف أن أصلهما المذكور ليس بصحيح لأن صحته تستلزم انتفاء الفاسد على مذهبنا واللازم باطل لأن الأحكام عندنا تنقسم إلى جائز وفاسد وباطل بيان الملازمة أن الربا مثلاً وسائر العقودات الفاسدة مشروعة بأصلها غير مشروعة بوصفها بالاتفاق وهي مما لا يقبل الإشارة. فلو كان ما ذكرناه صحيحاً لكان الأصل فيه مثل الوصف والوصف غير مشروع، وما كان غير مشروع بحسب الأصل والوصف فهو باطل اتفاقاً لا فاسد أو كان الوصف مثل الأصل والأصل مشروع فكان الربا جائزاً لا فاسداً . وهو باطل إجماعاً ا اهـ. قوله : (وما بقي لم يجز إلا بنية معينة مبيتة) أي ما بقي من الصيام وهو قضاء رمضان والكفارات وجزاء الصيد والحلق والمتعة والنذر المطلق لا يصح بمطلق النية ولا بنية مباينة، ولا بد فيه من التعيين لعدم تعين الوقت له ولا بد فيه أيضاً من النية من الليل أو ما هو في حكمه وهو المقارنة لطلوع الفجر بل هو الأصل لأن الواجب قرآن النية بالصوم لا تقديمها، وإنما جاز التقديم للضرورة ومن فروع لزوم التبييت في غير المعين لو نوى القضاء نهاراً فلم يصح هل يقع عن النفل في فتاوى النسفي نعم ولو أفطر يلزمه القضاء. قيل هذا إذا علم أن صومه عن لم يصح بنية من النهار، وأما إذا لم يعلم فلا يلزم بالشروع كما في المظنون. كذا في فتح القدير والذي يظهر ترجيح الإطلاق فإن الجهل بالأحكام في دار الإسلام ليس بمعتبر خصوصاً أن هذه المسألة ـ أعني عدم جواز القضاء بنية نهاراً - متفق عليها فيما يظهر فليس كالمظنون ولا يخفى أن قضاء النفل بعد إفساده وقضاء المنذور المعين داخل تحت قوله وما بقي ثم اعلم أن النية من الليل كافية في كل صوم بشرط عدم الرجوع عنها حتى لو نوى ليلاً أن غداً يصوم ثم عزم في الليل على الفطر لم يصبح صائماً، فلو أفطر لا شيء عليه إن لم يكن رمضان ولو مضى عليه لا يجزئه لأن تلك النية انتقضت بالرجوع ولو نوى الصائم الفطر لم يفطر حتى يأكل، وكذا لو نوى التكلم في الصلاة كذا في الظهيرية ولو قال نويت صوم غد إن شاء الله تعالى؛ فعن الحلواني يجوز استحساناً لأن المشيئة إنما تبطل اللفظ والنية فعل القلب وصححه في فتاوى الظهيرية
And the intention for voluntary (fasts) and what remains is not valid except with a specific intention made at night. Ramadan is established by sighting its crescent or after completing thirty days of Sha'ban for the resident... So whoever is not harmed by fasting is correct, and the discussion is not about that. Then know that it occurred in the statements of the scholars, both in principles and branches, that Ramadan is valid despite an error in description. So a group of mashayikh held that the issue of intending voluntary fasting in Ramadan is from the sound doubt: that one begins with this intention, then it appears that it is Ramadan, so it is excused. But if it occurs in other than that, one fears for him kufr because he thought the command for specific abstention is fulfilled by something else. In al-Taqrir it is mentioned. And in al-Nihaya it is refuted, as he said in the proof of al-Shafi'i: if one believes the prescribed act at this time to be voluntary, he becomes a disbeliever. And he said in its refutation that since voluntary sufficed him, the intention of aversion is not realized; thus his saying that if he believes it to be voluntary he becomes a disbeliever is invalidated. The conclusion: there is no necessary connection between intending voluntary and belief in non-obligation or its doubt; one may believe it obligatory yet intend voluntary, so he is not a disbeliever by intending voluntary unless accompanied by belief of voluntariness. Also, kufr is not feared unless accompanied by that doubt. Then know that Abu Hanifa followed his principle in all places that the original is separate from the description; thus he said: if the quality of obligation is invalidated in prayer, its original is not invalidated; if the quality is invalidated in fasting, its original remains; and if he says to her 'you are divorced as you wish', the original divorce occurs and the description is delegated to her. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said in this issue that what does not admit of pointing from among shari'i matters, its state and description are like its original, so the original is attached to its attachment. Thus they opposed this principle in fasting, and Abu Yusuf opposed him in prayer because he agreed with Abu Hanifa in it, and Muhammad acted on it in prayer, for he said the original is invalidated if the description is invalidated. Some differentiated for Muhammad between fasting and prayer, but al-Akmal refuted it in his Taqrir and said in his discussion: how is it that their mentioned principle is not sound because its soundness necessitates the absence of the fasid according to our madhhab, and the consequent is false because rulings according to us are divided into ja'iz, fasid, and batil. The demonstration: usury and all fasid contracts are legislated in their origin, not legislated in their description by agreement, and they are what do not admit of pointing. So if what we mentioned were sound, the origin would be like the description, and the description is non-legislated; what is non-legislated in both origin and description is batil by agreement, not fasid; or the description would be like the origin and the origin is legislated, so usury would be ja'iz, not fasid. That is invalid by consensus. His statement: 'and what remains is not valid except with a specific intention made at night' means what remains of fasting, which is make-up of Ramadan, expiations, compensation for hunting, shaving, tamattu', and absolute vows. It is not valid with absolute intention nor with contrary intention; specification is necessary because its time is not designated, and intention from the night or what is in its ruling is also necessary, which is being concomitant with the break of dawn; rather it is the original because the obligation is to attach intention to fasting, not to advance it; advancement is permitted only for necessity. Among the branches of the necessity of tabyit for non-designated: if one intends make-up during the day, it is not valid; does it fall as voluntary? In Fatawa al-Nasafi: yes, and if he breaks his fast, he must make it up. It is said: this is if he knows that his fast is not valid with daytime intention; but if he does not know, he is not bound by the commencement as in the doubtful. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and what appears is preferring the absolute, because ignorance of rulings in Dar al-Islam is not considered, especially since this issue—i.e., the impermissibility of make-up with daytime intention—is agreed upon apparently, so it is not like the doubtful. And it is not hidden that the make-up of voluntary after invalidating it and the make-up of designated vows are included under his statement 'what remains'. Then know that intention from the night is sufficient for every fast provided there is no revocation; so if one intends at night to fast tomorrow, then resolves at night to break it, he does not become a faster; if he breaks it, nothing is upon him if it is not Ramadan, and if the day passes, it does not suffice him because that intention was revoked by withdrawal. If the faster intends to break, he does not break until he eats; similarly if one intends to speak in prayer—so in al-Zahiriyya. If one says 'I intend to fast tomorrow if Allah wills', from al-Halwani: it is permissible by istihsan because the conditional only invalidates the wording, and intention is an act of the heart; and it was authenticated in Fatawa al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion probe on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the conditions for intention in fasting other than Ramadan. The author begins by stating the core ruling: voluntary fasts and what remains (i.e., make-up fasts, expiations, etc.) require a specific intention (ta'yin) made during the night (tabyit). However, if someone believes a prescribed act is voluntary in another context, that could lead to kufr because it implies rejection of the obligation. The commentator then enters a deep discussion on a principle of Abu Hanifa: the original act (asl) can be separated from its description (wasf). So if the description of obligation is invalid in prayer, the prayer itself remains valid; similarly in fasting. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differed: they held that in matters not subject to pointing, the description is like the origin, so invalidating the description invalidates the origin. This leads to intra-madhhab disagreements on specific cases. The commentator refutes the principle of the two companions by using the example of riba and fasid contracts, concluding that the principle is unsound. Returning to the matn, the author explains that 'what remains' includes qada of Ramadan, kaffarat, fidyah for hunting, shaving, tamattu', and absolute vows. Key Terms
الأصل ينفك عن الوصف (al-asl yanfakku 'an al-wasf): The original act can exist independently of its description or quality.
ما لا يقبل الإشارة (ma la yaqbil al-ishara): Matters that do not admit of pointing—i.e., intangible actions like prayer, fasting, contracts that cannot be physically indicated.
ربا (riba): Usury; a prohibited contract that is fasid in its description but its origin is legislated (i.e., the form of contract exists).
جائز وفاسد وباطل (ja'iz wa fasid wa batil): Ruling categories: ja'iz (valid and permissible), fasid (invalid in description but may have some effects), batil (void from inception).

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, what evidence is used to refute the principle of the two companions?
A) A hadith about intention.
B) An analogy with riba and fasid contracts.
C) The consensus of the companions.
D) The practice of the people of Madinah.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's principle that the original is separate from the description is sound and prevents the invalidation of acts due to minor intention errors.

Sawm — Fasting

Captive's Estimation of Ramadan and Required Make-up Fasts
Details the rulings for a captive estimating Ramadan and the required make-up fasts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a captive's estimated Ramadan fast to be valid, and calculate the required make-up days according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A captive's estimated fast is valid if it matches Ramadan or falls after it with proper intention; missing days due to Eid or month deficiency must be made up.

When a captive in enemy territory cannot determine the exact start of Ramadan, he may estimate and fast a month intending Ramadan. However, his fast's validity and the number of make-up days depend on how his estimated month relates to the actual Ramadan and other months. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details these conditional rulings, requiring careful parsing of multiple scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities for the estimated month relative to actual Ramadan
The text states the estimated month either agrees (wāfaqa), precedes (taqaddama), or follows (ta'akhkhara) the actual Ramadan. List these three outcomes.
These determine the basic ruling: validity or invalidity.
2
Determine the ruling for each possibility
If it agrees, valid; if it precedes, invalid; if it follows, valid with conditions. Write the three rulings.
Focus on the 'follows' case as it has detailed conditions.
3
Analyse the conditions for the 'follows' scenario when it coincides with Shawwal
When the estimated month falls on Shawwal, validity requires: (1) both months have same number of days, (2) specific intention (ta'yin), (3) pre-dawn intention (tabyīt). Then compute qada based on month lengths.
The text calculates qada for four sub-scenarios: both complete, Ramadan complete/Shawwal incomplete, etc.
4
Apply the same analysis to the scenario when it coincides with Dhul Hijjah
If the estimated month coincides with Dhul Hijjah, qada includes the day of Nahr (10th) and the three days of Tashriq (11th-13th). The text gives four sub-scenarios based on month lengths.
Note that qada may be 3, 4, or 5 days depending on the combination.
5
Handle the scenario of other months and cumulative years
If the estimated month coincides with any other month besides Shawwal and Dhul Hijjah, qada is only required if the estimated month is shorter than Ramadan (i.e., fewer days fasted). The closing lines address multiple years of estimation.
This completes the parsing of all conditional branches in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه يتفرع على كيفية النية ووقتها مسألة الأسير في دار الحرب إذا اشتبه عليه رمضان فتحرى وصام شهراً عن رمضان فلا يخلو إما أن يوافق أو لا بالتقديم أو بالتأخير، فإن وافق جاز، وإن تقدم لم يجز، وإن تأخر فإن وافق شوّالاً يجوز بشرط موافقة الشهرين في العدد وتعيين النية وتبييتها ولا يشترط نية القضاء في الصحيح فإن كان كل منهما كاملاً قضى يوماً واحداً لأجل يوم الفطر، وإن كان رمضان كاملاً وشوّال ناقصاً قضى يومين يوماً لأجل يوم العيد ويوماً لأجل النقصان وعلى العكس لا شيء عليه. وإن وافق صومه هلال ذي الحجة، فإن كان رمضان كاملاً وذو الحجة كاملاً قضى أربعة أيام النحر وأيام التشريق، وإن كان رمضان كاملاً وذو الحجة ناقصاً قضى خمسة أيام وعلى عکسه قضى ثلاثة أيام، وإن وافق صومه شهراً آخر سوى هذين الشهرين، فإن كان الشهران كاملين أو ناقصين أو كان رمضان ناقصاً والآخر كاملاً فلا شيء عليه، وعلى عكسه قضى يوماً. ولو صام بالتحري سنين كثيرة ثم تبين أنه صام في كل سنة قبل شهر يوم
And know that from the branches related to the how and time of intention is the issue of the captive in dar al-harb. If Ramadan is unclear to him, so he estimates and fasts a month as Ramadan, then it is either in agreement, or not—either being before or after. If it agrees, it is valid. If it is before, it is invalid. If it is after, and it coincides with Shawwal, it is valid on condition that the two months agree in number, with specific intention and tabyit, and intention for qada is not required according to the sound opinion. If both are complete, he makes up one day for the day of Fitr. If Ramadan is complete and Shawwal is incomplete, he makes up two days: one for the day of Eid and one for the deficiency. On the reverse, nothing is upon him. If his fast coincides with the new moon of Dhul Hijjah, then if Ramadan is complete and Dhul Hijjah is complete, he makes up four days: the day of Nahr and the days of Tashriq. If Ramadan is complete and Dhul Hijjah is incomplete, he makes up five days. On the reverse, he makes up three days. If his fast coincides with some other month besides these two, then if both months are complete or incomplete, or if Ramadan is incomplete and the other is complete, nothing is upon him. On the reverse, he makes up one day. And if he fasts by estimation for many years, then it becomes clear that he fasted each year a day before the month...
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise detailing a branch of fasting intention: the case of a captive in enemy territory (dar al-harb) who cannot determine the start of Ramadan. He is allowed to make an estimation (taharrī) and fast one month as Ramadan. The ruling depends on whether the month he fasts corresponds to the actual Ramadan, is before it, or after it. If it corresponds exactly, his fast is valid and no qada is needed. If it falls before, it is invalid and must be repeated. If it falls after, further conditions apply: if it coincides with Shawwal (the month after Ramadan), it is valid provided both months have the same number of days and he made specific and pre-dawn intention. In that case, he must make up the day of Eid (since fasting on Eid is forbidden). Detailed calculations follow for when months have different lengths. Similarly, if his fast coincides with Dhul Hijjah, he must make up the days of sacrifice and Tashriq. If it coincides with any other month, qada is only required if his estimated month is shorter than the actual Ramadan (i.e., he fasted fewer days). The passage ends with a scenario where this happens over many years.

Key Terms
تحرى (taharrā): The captive's effort to determine Ramadan by estimation or conjecture when the exact date is unknown.
أسير (asīr): A prisoner of war in enemy territory who is unable to know the exact beginning of Ramadan.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): The make-up fast for days that were not validly observed or were missed.
موافقة الشهرين في العدد (muwāfaqat al-shahrayn fi al-‘adad): The condition that the month fasted by the captive and the actual month (Ramadan) have the same number of days (29 or 30).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A captive estimates Ramadan and fasts a month that turns out to be after the actual Ramadan and coincides with Shawwal. If both months have 30 days, how many make-up days are required?
A) None
B) One day for Eid al-Fitr
C) Two days for Eid and deficiency
D) Four days for Eid and Tashriq

Key Takeaway
A captive's estimated fast is subject to detailed conditional rules; qada is required for days that fall on prohibited days or if the estimated month is shorter than Ramadan.

The Time of Intention for Fasting
Ruling statement on timing of intention for fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the timing of intention for obligatory and voluntary fasts, and apply the exceptions regarding qada, retraction, and conditional phrases.

Core Idea
Intention for obligatory fasts must be made before dawn; daytime intention counts only for voluntary fasts.

The timing of the intention (niyyah) is a critical element for the validity of a fast. This lesson presents the Hanafi view that for obligatory fasts, the intention must be made during the night, before dawn, while for voluntary fasts, daytime intention suffices. We will explore several practical branches, including the case of making a missed fast (qada) during the day, the effect of retracting the intention, and the ruling on adding 'in sha Allah' to the intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Recognize that the obligation is to join the intention with the dawn (tulu' al-fajr). Pre-dawn intention is permitted only out of necessity.
The text states: 'الواجب قرآن النية بالصوم لا تقديمها' — the wajib is to join intention with the fast, not to precede it.
2
Examine the branch of qada during the day
If someone intends qada (make-up) during the day, it is invalid for qada. However, according to Fatawa al-Nasafi, it counts as a voluntary (nafl) fast. Understand the consequence if broken.
Fatawa al-Nasafi rules that such a fast becomes nafl, and if broken, qada of that nafl is required.
3
Analyze the condition of non-retraction
Intention made at night is sufficient only if not retracted. If one retracts during the night, the intention is nullified and no fast is valid.
The text clarifies that retraction of intention (even without eating) negates the fast's validity.
4
Evaluate the effect of saying 'in sha Allah'
Adding a conditional phrase like 'in sha Allah' to the intention does not invalidate it by istihsan, because the conditional affects only the utterance, not the heart's intent.
This is from al-Halwani, authenticated in Fatawa al-Zahiriyya.
5
Apply the rulings to concrete scenarios
Test your understanding with the practice tasks that cover various combinations of intention timing, retraction, and conditional expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكمه وهو المقارنة لطلوع الفجر بل هو الأصل لأن الواجب قرآن النية بالصوم لا تقديمها، وإنما جاز التقديم للضرورة ومن فروع لزوم التبييت في غير المعين لو نوى القضاء نهاراً فلم يصح هل يقع عن النفل في فتاوى النسفي نعم ولو أفطر يلزمه القضاء. قيل هذا إذا علم أن صومه عن لم يصح بنية من النهار، وأما إذا لم يعلم فلا يلزم بالشروع كما في المظنون. كذا في فتح القدير والذي يظهر ترجيح الإطلاق فإن الجهل بالأحكام في دار الإسلام ليس بمعتبر خصوصاً أن هذه المسألة ـ أعني عدم جواز القضاء بنية نهاراً - متفق عليها فيما يظهر فليس كالمظنون ولا يخفى أن قضاء النفل بعد إفساده وقضاء المنذور المعين داخل تحت قوله وما بقي ثم اعلم أن النية من الليل كافية في كل صوم بشرط عدم الرجوع عنها حتى لو نوى ليلاً أن غداً يصوم ثم عزم في الليل على الفطر لم يصبح صائماً، فلو أفطر لا شيء عليه إن لم يكن رمضان ولو مضى عليه لا يجزئه لأن تلك النية انتقضت بالرجوع ولو نوى الصائم الفطر لم يفطر حتى يأكل، وكذا لو نوى التكلم في الصلاة كذا في الظهيرية ولو قال نويت صوم غد إن شاء الله تعالى؛ فعن الحلواني يجوز استحساناً لأن المشيئة إنما تبطل اللفظ والنية فعل القلب وصححه في فتاوى الظهيرية
Its rule: it is joining at dawn, and that is the original because the obligation is to join the intention with the fast, not to precede it. Preceding is only permitted out of necessity. Among the branches of the necessity of making intention at night for unspecified fasts: if one intends qada during the day, it is not valid. Is it counted as a voluntary fast? In Fatawa al-Nasafi: yes. And if he breaks it, qada is required. It was said: this is if he knows that his fast is not valid by intention from the daytime. But if he does not know, he is not bound by starting; as in al-Maznun. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and what appears is the preference of the absolute, because ignorance of rulings in Dar al-Islam is not considered, especially since this issue – i.e., the impermissibility of qada with intention during the day – is agreed upon apparently, so it is not like al-Maznun. It is not hidden that qada of a voluntary fast after corrupting it and qada of a specified vowed fast are included under his statement: and what remains. Then know that intention from the night is sufficient in every fast, on condition of not retracting from it. Even if one intends at night to fast tomorrow, then resolves during the night to break, he does not become a faster. If he breaks, nothing is upon him if it is not Ramadan, and if it passes, it does not suffice him because that intention was nullified by retraction. If the faster intends to break, he does not break until he eats. Similarly, if he intends to speak in prayer, as in al-Zahiriya. And if he says: I intend to fast tomorrow if Allah wills; from al-Halwani: it is permissible by istihsan because the conditional phrase only nullifies the utterance, and intention is an act of the heart; and it was authenticated in Fatawa al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the timing of intention for fasting. It begins by stating the ruling that the obligation is to have the intention concurrent with dawn (tulu' al-fajr), but pre-dawn intention is allowed due to necessity. The text then addresses several subsidiary issues: first, if one intends to make up a missed day (qada) during the day, it is invalid; however, according to Fatawa al-Nasafi, it counts as a voluntary fast. There is a difference of opinion on whether ignorance of this ruling excuses the person; the preferred view is that ignorance in Dar al-Islam is not excused because the ruling is well-known. Second, intention made at night is sufficient for all types of fasts (Ramadan, qada, nadhr, nafl) provided one does not retract it. If one retracts during the night, the intention is nullified, and if he breaks the fast later, no compensation is due unless it was Ramadan. Third, merely intending to break the fast does not invalidate it; actual eating or drinking is required. Fourth, adding 'in sha Allah' to the intention is permissible by istihsan because the conditional phrase affects the verbal expression, not the heart's intent. The passage clarifies that the heart's intention is the essence, and verbal utterances are secondary.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention of the heart to perform an act of worship, specifically fasting. It is the determinant of the type of fast (Ramadan, qada, nafl, etc.).
تبييت (tabyeet): Making the intention for fasting during the night, before dawn. For obligatory fasts, tabyeet is a condition (shart) for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says 'I intend to fast tomorrow, if Allah wills,' and then fasts, is his fast valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, because the condition nullifies the intention.
B) Yes, by istihsan, because the condition only affects the utterance, not the heart's intent.
C) No, because the intention must be absolute.
D) Yes, but only for voluntary fasts.

Key Takeaway
The intention for fasting is an act of the heart; for obligatory fasts it must be made before dawn, while daytime intention suffices for voluntary fasts, and conditional phrases do not harm the intention.
